style="color:blue">Ovary: Superior \xe2\x80\x93 3 fused carpels</span></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>\n<p><i><b>Narcissus</b></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> of predominantly spring <a href="/wiki/Perennial_plant" title="Perennial plant">perennial</a> plants of the <a href="/wiki/Amaryllidaceae" title="Amaryllidaceae">Amaryllidaceae</a> (amaryllis) family. Various common names including <b>daffodil</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;Note 1&#93;</a></sup> <b>daffadowndilly</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <b>narcissus</b> and <b>jonquil</b> are used to describe all or some members of the genus. <i>Narcissus</i> has conspicuous flowers with six petal-like <a href="/wiki/Tepals" class="mw-redirect" title="Tepals">tepals</a> surmounted by a cup- or trumpet-shaped <a href="/wiki/Corona_(plant_structure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Corona (plant structure)">corona</a>. The flowers are generally white or yellow (also orange or pink in garden varieties), with either uniform or contrasting coloured tepals and corona.\n</p><p><i>Narcissus</i> were well known in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_civilisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient civilisation">ancient civilisation</a>, both medicinally and botanically, but formally described by <a href="/wiki/Linnaeus" class="mw-redirect" title="Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Species_Plantarum" title="Species Plantarum">Species Plantarum</a></i> (1753). The genus is generally considered to have about ten sections with approximately 50 species. The number of species has varied, depending on how they are classified, due to similarity between species and <a href="/wiki/Hybridization_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hybridization (biology)">hybridisation</a>. The genus arose some time in the Late <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> to Early <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> epochs, in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> and adjacent areas of southwest Europe. The exact origin of the name <i>Narcissus</i> is unknown, but it is often linked to a Greek word for intoxicated (<a href="/wiki/Narcotic" title="Narcotic">narcotic</a>) and the myth of the <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(mythology)" title="Narcissus (mythology)">youth of that name</a> who fell in love with his own reflection. The English word "daffodil" appears to be derived from "<a href="/wiki/Asphodelus" title="Asphodelus">asphodel</a>", with which it was commonly compared.\n</p><p>The species are native to meadows and woods in southern Europe and North Africa with a <a href="/wiki/Centre_of_diversity" class="mw-redirect" title="Centre of diversity">centre of diversity</a> in the Western Mediterranean, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a>. Both wild and cultivated plants have naturalised widely, and were introduced into the Far East prior to the tenth century. Narcissi tend to be long-lived bulbs, which propagate by division, but are also insect-pollinated. Known pests, diseases and disorders include viruses, fungi, the larvae of flies, <a href="/wiki/Mites" class="mw-redirect" title="Mites">mites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nematodes" class="mw-redirect" title="Nematodes">nematodes</a>. Some <i>Narcissus</i> species have become extinct, while others are threatened by increasing urbanisation and tourism.\n</p><p>Historical accounts suggest narcissi have been cultivated from the earliest times, but became increasingly popular in Europe after the 16th century and by the late 19th century were an important commercial crop centred primarily on the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. Today narcissi are popular as cut flowers and as ornamental plants in private and public gardens. The long history of breeding has resulted in thousands of different <a href="/wiki/Cultivars" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultivars">cultivars</a>. For horticultural purposes, narcissi are classified into divisions, covering a wide range of shapes and colours. Like other members of their family, narcissi produce a number of different <a href="/wiki/Alkaloids" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkaloids">alkaloids</a>, which provide some protection for the plant, but may be poisonous if accidentally ingested. This property has been exploited for medicinal use in traditional healing and has resulted in the production of <a href="/wiki/Galantamine" title="Galantamine">galantamine</a> for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer&#39;s disease">Alzheimer\'s dementia</a>. Long celebrated in art and literature, narcissi are associated with a number of themes in different cultures, ranging from death to good fortune, and as symbols of spring. The daffodil is the national flower of <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and the symbol of cancer charities in many countries. The appearance of the wild flowers in spring is associated with festivals in many places.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Vegetative</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage"><a data-file-height="3195" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Narcissus</i> <a href="/wiki/Capsule_(botany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Capsule (botany)">capsule</a> dispersing <b><a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seed</a></b></div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General">General</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: General">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Narcissus</i> is a genus of <a href="/wiki/Perennial" class="mw-redirect" title="Perennial">perennial</a> <a href="/wiki/Herbaceous" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbaceous">herbaceous</a> <a href="/wiki/Bulb" title="Bulb">bulbiferous</a> <a href="/wiki/Geophytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Geophytes">geophytes</a>, dying back after flowering to an underground storage bulb. They regrow in the following year from brown-skinned ovoid <a href="/wiki/Bulbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Bulbs">bulbs</a> with pronounced necks, and reach heights of 5\xe2\x80\x9380&#160;cm depending on the species. Dwarf species such as <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_asturiensis" title="Narcissus asturiensis">N. asturiensis</a></i> have a maximum height of 5\xe2\x80\x938&#160;cm, while <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_tazetta" title="Narcissus tazetta">Narcissus tazetta</a></i> may grow as tall as 80&#160;cm.<sup id="cite_ref-Tazetta_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tazetta-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The plants are <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#S" title="Glossary of botanical terms">scapose</a>, having a single central leafless hollow flower <a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">stem</a> (scape). Several green or blue-green, narrow, strap-shaped leaves arise from the bulb. The plant stem usually bears a solitary flower, but occasionally a cluster of flowers (<a href="/wiki/Umbel" title="Umbel">umbel</a>). The flowers, which are usually conspicuous and white or yellow, sometimes both or rarely green, consist of a <a href="/wiki/Perianth" title="Perianth">perianth</a> of three parts. Closest to the stem (proximal) is a <a href="/wiki/Petal#Variations" title="Petal">floral tube</a> above the <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">ovary</a>, then an outer ring composed of six <a href="/wiki/Tepals" class="mw-redirect" title="Tepals">tepals</a> (undifferentiated sepals and petals), and a central disc to conical shaped <a href="/wiki/Corona_(plant_structure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Corona (plant structure)">corona</a>. The flowers may hang down (pendent), or be erect. There are six <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> bearing <a href="/wiki/Stamens" class="mw-redirect" title="Stamens">stamens</a> surrounding a central <a href="/wiki/Style_(flower)" class="mw-redirect" title="Style (flower)">style</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">ovary</a> is inferior (below the floral parts) consisting of three chambers (trilocular). The <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> consists of a dry <a href="/wiki/Capsule_(botany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Capsule (botany)">capsule</a> that splits (<a href="/wiki/Dehisces" class="mw-redirect" title="Dehisces">dehisces</a>) releasing numerous black <a href="/wiki/Seeds" class="mw-redirect" title="Seeds">seeds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The bulb lies dormant after the leaves and flower stem die back and has contractile <a href="/wiki/Root" title="Root">roots</a> that pull it down further into the soil. The flower stem and leaves form in the bulb, to emerge the following season. Most species are dormant from summer to late winter, flowering in the spring, though a few species are autumn flowering.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specific">Specific</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Specific">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vegetative">Vegetative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vegetative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Bulbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Bulbs">Bulbs</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p>The pale brown-skinned <a href="/wiki/Ovoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ovoid">ovoid</a> <a href="/wiki/Tunica_(biology)" title="Tunica (biology)">tunicate</a> <b><a href="/wiki/Bulbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Bulbs">bulbs</a></b> have a membranous tunic and a corky stem (base or basal) plate from which arise the <a href="/wiki/Adventitious#roots" class="mw-redirect" title="Adventitious">adventitious</a> <a href="/wiki/Root_hairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Root hairs">root hairs</a> in a ring around the edge, which grow up to 40&#160;mm in length. Above the stem plate is the storage organ consisting of bulb scales, surrounding the previous flower stalk and the terminal <a href="/wiki/Bud" title="Bud">bud</a>. The scales are of two types, true storage organs and the bases of the foliage leaves. These have a thicker tip and a scar from where the leaf lamina became detached. The innermost leaf scale is semicircular only partly enveloping the flower stalk (semisheathed).(see Hanks Fig 1.3). The bulb may contain a number of branched bulb units, each with two to three true scales and two to three leaf bases. Each bulb unit has a life of about four years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi&#93;,_p._104_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi],_p._104-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Once the leaves die back in summer, the roots also wither. After some years, the roots shorten pulling the bulbs deeper into the ground (<a href="/wiki/Adventitious_roots" class="mw-redirect" title="Adventitious roots">contractile roots</a>). The bulbs develop from the inside, pushing the older layers outwards which become brown and dry, forming an outer shell, the tunic or skin. Up to 60 layers have been counted in some wild species. While the plant appears dormant above the ground the flower stalk which will start to grow in the following spring, develops within the bulb surrounded by two to three deciduous leaves and their sheaths. The flower stem lies in the <a href="/wiki/Axil" class="mw-redirect" title="Axil">axil</a> of the second true leaf.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">Stems</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p>The single leafless <b><a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">stem</a></b> or scape, appearing from early to late spring depending on the species, bears from 1 to 20 blooms.<sup id="cite_ref-RHSAZ_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSAZ-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Stem shape depends on the species, some are highly compressed with a visible seam, while others are rounded. The stems are upright and located at the centre of the leaves. In a few species such as <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_hedraeanthus" title="Narcissus hedraeanthus">N. hedraeanthus</a></i> the stem is oblique (asymmetrical). The stem is hollow in the upper portion but towards the bulb is more solid and filled with a spongy material.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStraleyUtech2003_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStraleyUtech2003-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Leaves" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaves">Leaves</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p><i>Narcissus</i> plants have one to several basal <b><a href="/wiki/Leaves" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaves">leaves</a></b> which are linear, ligulate or strap shaped (long and narrow), sometimes <a href="/wiki/Caespitose" class="mw-redirect" title="Caespitose">channelled</a> <a href="/wiki/Acaulescent" class="mw-redirect" title="Acaulescent">adaxially</a> to <a href="/wiki/Leaf_shape" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaf shape">semiterete</a>, and may (pedicellate) or may not (sessile) have a <a href="/wiki/Petiole_(botany)" title="Petiole (botany)">petiole</a> stalk.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus&#93;,_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus],_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The leaves are flat and broad to cylindrical at the base and arise from the bulb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus._p._285&#93;_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus._p._285]-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The emerging plant generally has two leaves, but the mature plant usually three, rarely four, and they are covered with a <a href="/wiki/Cutin" title="Cutin">cutin</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticle</a>, giving them a waxy appearance. Leaf colour is light green to blue-green. In the mature plant the leaves extend higher than the flower stem, but in some species the leaves are low hanging. The leaf base is encased in a colourless sheath. After flowering the leaves turn yellow and die back once the <a href="/wiki/Seed_pod" class="mw-redirect" title="Seed pod">seed pod</a> (fruit) is ripe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jonquils usually have dark green, round, rush-like leaves.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive">Reproductive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reproductive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">Inflorescence</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p>The <b>inflorescence</b> is scapose, the single <a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">stem</a> or scape bearing either a solitary flower or forming an <a href="/wiki/Umbel" title="Umbel">umbel</a> with up to 20 blooms.<sup id="cite_ref-RHSAZ_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSAZ-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Species bearing a solitary flower include section <i>Bulbocodium</i> and most of section <i>Pseudonarcissus</i>. <a href="/wiki/Umbellate" class="mw-redirect" title="Umbellate">Umbellate</a> species have a fleshy <a href="/wiki/Racemose" class="mw-redirect" title="Racemose">racemose</a> <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a> (unbranched, with short floral stalks) with 2 to 15 or 20 flowers, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_papyraceus" title="Narcissus papyraceus">N. papyraceus</a></i> (see illustration, left) and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_tazetta" title="Narcissus tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i> (see <a href="#Subdivision">Table I</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FE5_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FE5-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The flower arrangement on the inflorescence may be either with (<a href="/wiki/Pedicel_(botany)" title="Pedicel (botany)">pedicellate</a>) or without (<a href="/wiki/Sessility_(botany)" title="Sessility (botany)">sessile</a>) floral stalks.\n</p><p>Prior to opening, the flower buds are enveloped and protected in a thin dry papery or membranous (<a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#S" title="Glossary of botanical terms">scarious</a>) <a href="/wiki/Spathe" class="mw-redirect" title="Spathe">spathe</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Spathe" class="mw-redirect" title="Spathe">spathe</a> consists of a singular <a href="/wiki/Bract" title="Bract">bract</a> that is ribbed, and which remains wrapped around the base of the open flower. As the bud grows, the spathe splits longitudinally.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus&#93;,_p._285_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus],_p._285-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnston2007_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnston2007-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bracteoles" class="mw-redirect" title="Bracteoles">Bracteoles</a> are small or absent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStraleyUtech2003_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStraleyUtech2003-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus&#93;,_p._285_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus],_p._285-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FE5_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FE5-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Flowers" class="mw-redirect" title="Flowers">Flowers</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Flowers" class="mw-redirect" title="Flowers">flowers</a></b> of <i>Narcissus</i>  are <a href="/wiki/Hermaphroditic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hermaphroditic">hermaphroditic</a> (bisexual),<sup id="cite_ref-Medrano_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medrano-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> have three parts (tripartite), and are sometimes <a href="/wiki/Fragrant" class="mw-redirect" title="Fragrant">fragrant</a> (see <a href="#Fragrances">Fragrances</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDobson1997_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDobson1997-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The flower symmetry is <a href="/wiki/Actinomorphic" class="mw-redirect" title="Actinomorphic">actinomorphic</a> (radial) to slightly zygomorphic (bilateral) due to declinate-ascending <a href="/wiki/Stamens" class="mw-redirect" title="Stamens">stamens</a> (curving downwards, then bent up at the tip). <i>Narcissus</i> flowers are characterised by their, usually conspicuous, <a href="/wiki/Corona_(plant_structure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Corona (plant structure)">corona</a> (trumpet).\n</p><p>The three major floral parts (in all species except <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_cavanillesii" title="Narcissus cavanillesii">N. cavanillesii</a></i> in which the corona is virtually absent - <a href="#Subdivision">Table I</a>: Section <i>Tapeinanthus</i>) are;\n</p>\n<ul><li>(i) the proximal <a href="/wiki/Floral_tube" class="mw-redirect" title="Floral tube">floral tube</a> (hypanthium),</li>\n<li>(ii) the surrounding free <a href="/wiki/Tepals" class="mw-redirect" title="Tepals">tepals</a>, and</li>\n<li>(iii) the more distal <a href="/wiki/Corona_(plant_structure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Corona (plant structure)">corona</a> (paraperigon, paraperigonium).</li></ul>\n<p>All three parts may be considered to be components of the <a href="/wiki/Perianth" title="Perianth">perianth</a> (perigon, perigonium). The <a href="/wiki/Perianth" title="Perianth">perianth</a> arises above the apex of the inferior <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">ovary</a>, its base forming the hypanthial <a href="/wiki/Floral_tube" class="mw-redirect" title="Floral tube">floral tube</a>.\n</p><p>The floral tube is formed by fusion of the basal segments of the tepals (proximally connate). Its shape is from an inverted cone (<a href="/wiki/Obconic" title="Obconic">obconic</a>) to funnel-shaped (funneliform) or cylindrical, and is surmounted by the more distal corona. Floral tubes can range from long and narrow in sections <i>Apodanthi</i> and <i>Jonquilla</i> to rudimentary (<i>N. cavanillesii</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA343_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae)&#93;,_p._343_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA343_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae)],_p._343-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Surrounding the floral tube and corona and <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#reflexed" title="Glossary of botanical terms">reflexed</a> (bent back) from the rest of the perianth are the six spreading tepals or floral leaves, in two whorls which may be distally ascending, reflexed (folded back), or lanceolate. Like many <a href="/wiki/Monocotyledons" class="mw-redirect" title="Monocotyledons">monocotyledons</a>, the perianth is homochlamydeous, that is undifferentiated into separate calyx (sepals) and corolla (petals), but rather has six tepals. The three outer tepal segments may be considered <a href="/wiki/Sepal" title="Sepal">sepals</a>, and the three inner segments <a href="/wiki/Petal" title="Petal">petals</a>. The transition point between the floral tube and the corona is marked by the insertion of the free tepals on the fused perianth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi&#93;,_p._104_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi],_p._104-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The corona, or paracorolla, is variously described as bell-shaped (funneliform, trumpet), bowl-shaped (cupular, crateriform, cup shaped) or disc-shaped with margins that are often frilled, and is free from the stamens. Rarely the corona is a simple callose (hardened, thickened) ring. The corona is formed during floral development as a tubular outgrowth from stamens which fuse into a tubular structure, the anthers becoming reduced. At its base the fragrances which attract pollinators are formed. All species produce nectar at the top of the ovary.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Coronal morphology varies from the tiny pigmented disk of <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_serotinus" title="Narcissus serotinus">N. serotinus</a></i> (see <a href="#Subdivision">Table I</a>) or the rudimentary structure in <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_cavanillesii" title="Narcissus cavanillesii">N. cavanillesii</a></i> to the elongated trumpets of section <i>Pseudonarcissus</i> (trumpet daffodils, Table I).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus&#93;,_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus],_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FE5_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FE5-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi&#93;,_p._104_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi],_p._104-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the perianth may point forwards, in some species such as <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_cyclamineus" title="Narcissus cyclamineus">N. cyclamineus</a></i> it is folded back (reflexed, see illustration, left), while in some other species such as <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_bulbocodium" title="Narcissus bulbocodium">N. bulbocodium</a></i> (<a href="#Subdivision">Table I</a>), it is reduced to a few barely visible pointed segments with a prominent corona.\n</p><p>The colour of the perianth is white, yellow or bicoloured, with the exception of the night flowering <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_viridiflorus" title="Narcissus viridiflorus">N. viridiflorus</a></i>  which is green. In addition the corona of <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_poeticus" title="Narcissus poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i> has a red crenulate margin (see <a href="#Subdivision">Table I</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus._p._285&#93;_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus._p._285]-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Flower diameter varies from 12&#160;mm <i>(<a href="/wiki/N._bulbocodium" class="mw-redirect" title="N. bulbocodium">N. bulbocodium</a>)</i> to over 125&#160;mm (<i>N. nobilis</i>=<i><a href="/wiki/N._pseudonarcissus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. pseudonarcissus">N. pseudonarcissus</a></i> supsp. <i>nobilis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA343_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._&quot;Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly&quot;&#93;_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae),_p._343_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA343_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._&quot;Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly&quot;]_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae),_p._343-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Flower orientation varies from pendent or deflexed (hanging down) as in <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_triandrus" title="Narcissus triandrus">N. triandrus</a></i> (see illustration, left), through declinate-ascendant as in <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_pseudonarcissus" title="Narcissus pseudonarcissus"><i>N. alpestris</i> = <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> subsp. <i>moschatus</i></a>, horizontal (patent, spreading) such as <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_gaditanus" title="Narcissus gaditanus">N. gaditanus</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_poeticus" title="Narcissus poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i>, erect as in <i>N. cavanillesii</i>, <i>N. serotinus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/N._rupicola" class="mw-redirect" title="N. rupicola">N. rupicola</a></i> (<a href="#Subdivision">Table I</a>), or intermediate between these positions (erecto-patent).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStraleyUtech2003_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStraleyUtech2003-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus._p._285&#93;_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESellMurrell1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpKGlBap0FRYClpgPA285pgPA285vonepageqffalse_Narcissus._p._285]-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FE5_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FE5-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA343_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._&quot;Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly&quot;&#93;_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae),_p._343_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA343_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._&quot;Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly&quot;]_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae),_p._343-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The flowers of <i>Narcissus</i> demonstrate exceptional floral diversity and sexual <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">polymorphism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> primarily by corona size and floral tube length, associated with <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinator</a> groups (see for instance Figs. 1 and 2 in Graham and Barrett<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>). Barrett and Harder (2005) describe three separate floral patterns;\n</p>\n<ul><li>"Daffodil" form</li>\n<li>"Paperwhite" form</li>\n<li>"Triandrus" form.</li></ul>\n<p>The predominant patterns are the \'daffodil\' and \'paperwhite\' forms, while the "triandrus" form is less common. Each corresponds to a different group of pollinators (See <a href="#Pollination">Pollination</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The "daffodil" form, which includes sections <i>Pseudonarcissus</i> and <i>Bulbocodium</i>, has a relatively short, broad or highly funnelform tube (funnel-like), which grades into an elongated corona, which is large and funnelform, forming a broad, cylindrical or trumpet-shaped perianth. Section <i>Pseudonarcissus</i> consists of relatively large flowers with a corolla length of around 50mm, generally solitary but rarely in inflorescences of 2\xe2\x80\x934 flowers. They have wide greenish floral tubes with funnel shaped bright yellow coronas. The six tepals sometimes differ in colour from the corona and may be cream coloured to pale yellow.<sup id="cite_ref-Medrano_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medrano-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The "paperwhite" form, including sections <i>Jonquilla</i>, <i>Apodanthi</i> and <i>Narcissus</i>, has a relatively long, narrow tube and a short, shallow, flaring corona. The flower is horizontal and fragrant.\n</p><p>The "triandrus" form is seen in only two species, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_albimarginatus" title="Narcissus albimarginatus">N. albimarginatus</a></i> (a Moroccan endemic) and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_triandrus" title="Narcissus triandrus">N. triandrus</a></i>. It combines features of both the "daffodil" and "paperwhite" forms, with a well-developed, long, narrow tube and an extended bell-shaped corona of almost equal length. The flowers are pendent.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Androecium" class="mw-redirect" title="Androecium">Androecium</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p>There are six <a href="/wiki/Stamens" class="mw-redirect" title="Stamens">stamens</a> in one to two rows (<a href="/wiki/Whorl_(botany)" title="Whorl (botany)">whorls</a>), with the filaments separate from the corona, attached at the throat or base of the tube (epipetalous), often of two separate lengths, straight or declinate-ascending (curving downwards, then upwards). The anthers are basifixed (attached at their base).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus&#93;,_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus],_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi&#93;,_p._104_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi],_p._104-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Gynoecium" title="Gynoecium">Gynoecium</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">ovary</a> is inferior (below the floral parts) and <a href="/wiki/Locule" title="Locule">trilocular</a> (three chambered) and there is a <a href="/wiki/Pistil" class="mw-redirect" title="Pistil">pistil</a> with a minutely three lobed <a href="/wiki/Stigma_(botany)" title="Stigma (botany)">stigma</a> and filiform (thread like) <a href="/wiki/Style_(flower)" class="mw-redirect" title="Style (flower)">style</a>, which is often exserted (extending beyond the tube).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101&#93;_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101]-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi&#93;,_p._104_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi],_p._104-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">Fruit</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p>The fruit consists of dehiscent <a href="/wiki/Loculicidal" class="mw-redirect" title="Loculicidal">loculicidal</a> <a href="/wiki/Capsule_(botany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Capsule (botany)">capsules</a> (splitting between the locules) that are <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoid</a> to <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#S" title="Glossary of botanical terms">subglobose</a> (almost spherical) in shape and are papery to leathery in texture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStraleyUtech2003_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStraleyUtech2003-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Seeds" class="mw-redirect" title="Seeds">Seeds</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p>The fruit contains numerous  subglobose <b><a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a></b> which are round and swollen with a hard coat, sometimes with an attached <a href="/wiki/Elaiosome" title="Elaiosome">elaiosome</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Seed#Seed_coat" title="Seed">testa</a> is black<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus&#93;,_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus],_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Pericarp" class="mw-redirect" title="Pericarp">pericarp</a> dry.<sup id="cite_ref-FE5_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FE5-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most species have 12 <a href="/wiki/Ovules" class="mw-redirect" title="Ovules">ovules</a> and 36 seeds, although some species such as <i>N. bulbocodium</i> have more, up to a maximum of 60. Seeds take five to six weeks to mature. The seeds of sections <i>Jonquilla</i> and <i>Bulbocodium</i> are wedge-shaped and matte black, while those of other sections are ovate and glossy black. A gust of wind or contact with a passing animal is sufficient to <a href="/wiki/Seed_dispersal" title="Seed dispersal">disperse</a> the mature seeds.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromosomes">Chromosomes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Chromosomes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">Chromosome</a> numbers include 2n=14, 22, 26, with numerous <a href="/wiki/Aneuploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Aneuploid">aneuploid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyploid">polyploid</a> derivatives. The basic chromosome number is 7, with the exception of <i><a href="/wiki/N._tazetta" class="mw-redirect" title="N. tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_elegans" title="Narcissus elegans">N. elegans</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/N._broussonetii" class="mw-redirect" title="N. broussonetii">N. broussonetii</a></i> in which it is 10 or 11; this subgenus (<i>Hermione</i>) was in fact characterised by this characteristic. Polyploid species include <i><a href="/wiki/N._papyraceus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. papyraceus">N. papyraceus</a></i> (4x=22) and <i><a href="/wiki/N._dubius" class="mw-redirect" title="N. dubius">N. dubius</a></i> (6x=50).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi&#93;,_p._104_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi],_p._104-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytochemistry">Phytochemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Phytochemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alkaloids">Alkaloids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Alkaloids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>As with all Amarylidaceae genera, <i>Narcissus</i> contains unique <a href="/wiki/Isoquinoline" title="Isoquinoline">isoquinoline</a> <a href="/wiki/Alkaloids" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkaloids">alkaloids</a>. The first alkaloid to be identified was <a href="/wiki/Lycorine" title="Lycorine">lycorine</a>, from <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> in 1877. These are considered a protective adaptation, and are utilised in the classification of species. Nearly 100 alkaloids have been identified in the genus, about a third of all known Amaryllidaceae alkaloids, although not all species have been tested. Of the nine alkaloid <a href="/wiki/Ring_(chemistry)" title="Ring (chemistry)">ring</a> types identified in the family, <i>Narcissus</i> species most commonly demonstrate the presence of alkaloids from within the Lycorine (lycorine, galanthine, pluviine) and <a href="/wiki/Homolycorine" title="Homolycorine">Homolycorine</a> (homolycorine, lycorenine) groups. Hemanthamine, Tazettine, Narciclasine, Montanine and <a href="/wiki/Galantamine" title="Galantamine">Galantamine</a> alkaloids are also represented. The alkaloid profile of any plant varies with time, location, and developmental stage.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <i>Narcissus</i> also contain <a href="/wiki/Fructans" class="mw-redirect" title="Fructans">fructans</a> and low molecular weight <a href="/wiki/Glucomannan" title="Glucomannan">glucomannan</a> in the leaves and plant stems.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fragrances">Fragrances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fragrances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Fragrances" class="mw-redirect" title="Fragrances">Fragrances</a> are predominantly <a href="/wiki/Monoterpene" title="Monoterpene">monoterpene</a> <a href="/wiki/Isoprenoids" class="mw-redirect" title="Isoprenoids">isoprenoids</a>, with a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Benzenoids" class="mw-redirect" title="Benzenoids">benzenoids</a>, although <i><a href="/wiki/N._jonquilla" class="mw-redirect" title="N. jonquilla">N. jonquilla</a></i> has both equally represented. Another exception is <i><a href="/wiki/N._cuatrecasasii" class="mw-redirect" title="N. cuatrecasasii">N. cuatrecasasii</a></i> which produces mainly fatty acid derivatives. The basic monoterpene precursor is <a href="/wiki/Geranyl_pyrophosphate" title="Geranyl pyrophosphate">geranyl pyrophosphate</a>, and the commonest monoterpenes are <a href="/wiki/Limonene" title="Limonene">limonene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myrcene" title="Myrcene">myrcene</a>, and <i>trans</i>-\xce\xb2-<a href="/wiki/Ocimene" title="Ocimene">ocimene</a>. Most benzenoids are non-methoxylated, while a few species contain <a href="/wiki/Methoxylated" class="mw-redirect" title="Methoxylated">methoxylated</a> forms (<a href="/wiki/Ethers" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethers">ethers</a>), <i>e.g.</i>  <i><a href="/wiki/N._bugei" class="mw-redirect" title="N. bugei">N. bugei</a></i>. Other ingredient include <a href="/wiki/Indole" title="Indole">indole</a>, isopentenoids and very small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Sesquiterpenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesquiterpenes">sesquiterpenes</a>. Fragrance patterns can be correlated with <a href="/wiki/Pollinators" class="mw-redirect" title="Pollinators">pollinators</a>, and fall into three main groups (see <a href="#Pollination">Pollination</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDobson1997_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDobson1997-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_of_Narcissus" title="Taxonomy of Narcissus">Taxonomy of Narcissus</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><i>Genus valde intricatum et numerosissimis dubiis oppressum</i><br /> A genus that is very complex and burdened with numerous uncertainties</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Schultes &amp; Schultes fil., <i>Syst. Veg.</i> 1829<sup id="cite_ref-Schultes_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schultes-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early">Early</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Early">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The genus <i>Narcissus</i> was well known to the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">ancients</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> literature <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a><sup id="cite_ref-Theophrastus_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Theophrastus-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Dioscorides" class="mw-redirect" title="Dioscorides">Dioscorides</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dioscurides_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dioscurides-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> described <i>\xce\xbd\xce\xac\xcf\x81\xce\xba\xce\xb9\xcf\x83\xcf\x83\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</i>, probably referring to <i><a href="/wiki/N._poeticus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i>, although the exact species mentioned in classical literature cannot be accurately established. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> later introduced the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> form <i>narcissus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-PlinyLat_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PlinyLat-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pliny_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pliny-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PlinyLat2_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PlinyLat2-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pliny2_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pliny2-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>  These early writers were as much interested in the plant\'s possible medicinal properties as they were its botanical features and their accounts remained influential until at least the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> (see also <a href="#Antiquity">Antiquity</a>). Mediaeval and Renaissance writers include <a href="/wiki/Albert_Magnus" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert Magnus">Albert Magnus</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Turner_(naturalist)" title="William Turner (naturalist)">William Turner</a>, but it remained to <a href="/wiki/Linnaeus" class="mw-redirect" title="Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a> to formally describe and name <i>Narcissus</i> as a genus in his <a href="/wiki/Species_Plantarum" title="Species Plantarum">Species Plantarum</a> (1753) at which time there were six known species.<sup id="cite_ref-LinSP_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LinSP-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meerow_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meerow-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Antoine_Laurent_de_Jussieu" title="Antoine Laurent de Jussieu">De Jussieu</a> (1789) grouped <i>Narcissus</i> into a "family",<sup id="cite_ref-icn18.2_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-icn18.2-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Candolle2_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Candolle2-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> which he called Narcissi.<sup id="cite_ref-Jussieu_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jussieu-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This was renamed Amaryllideae by <a href="/wiki/Jaume_Saint-Hilaire" class="mw-redirect" title="Jaume Saint-Hilaire">Jaume Saint-Hilaire</a> in 1805,<sup id="cite_ref-JSH_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JSH-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> corresponding to the modern <a href="/wiki/Amaryllidaceae" title="Amaryllidaceae">Amaryllidaceae</a>. For a while <i>Narcissus</i> was considered part of <a href="/wiki/Liliaceae" title="Liliaceae">Liliaceae</a> (as in the illustration seen here of <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_candidissimus" class="mw-redirect" title="Narcissus candidissimus">Narcissus candidissimus</a></i>),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERedout\xc3\xa9De_Candolle1805\xe2\x80\x931808&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem10268page56mode1up_vol._IV,_p._188&#93;_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERedout\xc3\xa9De_Candolle1805\xe2\x80\x931808[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem10268page56mode1up_vol._IV,_p._188]-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Candollea_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Candollea-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Candolleb_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Candolleb-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> but then the Amaryllidaceae were split off from it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEA._P._de_Candolle1813219_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEA._P._de_Candolle1813219-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1810&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem21871page164mode1up_Prodromus&#93;,_p._296_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1810[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem21871page164mode1up_Prodromus],_p._296-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_candidissimus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Narcissus_candidissimus.jpg/220px-Narcissus_candidissimus.jpg" width="220" height="348" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Narcissus_candidissimus.jpg/330px-Narcissus_candidissimus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Narcissus_candidissimus.jpg/440px-Narcissus_candidissimus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="989" data-file-height="1566" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_candidissimus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_candidissimus" class="mw-redirect" title="Narcissus candidissimus">N. candidissimus</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Redout%C3%A9" title="Pierre-Joseph Redout\xc3\xa9">Pierre-Joseph Redout\xc3\xa9</a>, 1808<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERedout\xc3\xa9De_Candolle1805\xe2\x80\x931808&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem10268page56mode1up_vol._IV,_p._188&#93;_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERedout\xc3\xa9De_Candolle1805\xe2\x80\x931808[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem10268page56mode1up_vol._IV,_p._188]-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Various authors have adopted either narrow (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Hardy_Haworth" title="Adrian Hardy Haworth">Haworth</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Haworth_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haworth-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BurbigeHaworth_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BurbigeHaworth-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Anthony_Salisbury" title="Richard Anthony Salisbury">Salisbury</a><sup id="cite_ref-Salisbury_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salisbury-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>) or wide (e.g.<a href="/wiki/William_Herbert_(botanist)" title="William Herbert (botanist)">Herbert</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Herbert_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Spach" title="\xc3\x89douard Spach">Spach</a><sup id="cite_ref-Spach_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spach-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> ) interpretations of the genus.<sup id="cite_ref-RHSSegregate_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSSegregate-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>  The narrow view treated many of the species as separate genera.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebb1978_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebb1978-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Over time the wider view prevailed with a major monograph on the genus being published by <a href="/wiki/John_Gilbert_Baker" title="John Gilbert Baker">Baker</a> (1875).<sup id="cite_ref-Baker_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> One of the more controversial genera was <i>Tapeinanthus</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Cullen_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cullen-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebb1978_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebb1978-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> but today it is included in <i>Narcissus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The eventual position of <i>Narcissus</i> within the <a href="/wiki/Amaryllidaceae" title="Amaryllidaceae">Amaryllidaceae</a> <a href="/wiki/Family_(botany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Family (botany)">family</a> only became settled in this century with the advent of <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_analysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogenetic analysis">phylogenetic analysis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Angiosperm_Phylogeny_Group" title="Angiosperm Phylogeny Group">Angiosperm Phylogeny Group</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-Meerow_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meerow-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStevens2015_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStevens2015-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Within Amaryllidaceae the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i>Narcissus</i> belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Narcisseae" title="Narcisseae">Narcisseae</a> <a href="/wiki/Tribe_(botany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tribe (botany)">tribe</a>, one of 13 within the <a href="/wiki/Amaryllidoideae" title="Amaryllidoideae">Amaryllidoideae</a> <a href="/wiki/Subfamily" title="Subfamily">subfamily</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> It is one of two <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister</a> <a href="/wiki/Clades" class="mw-redirect" title="Clades">clades</a> corresponding to genera in the Narcisseae,<sup id="cite_ref-Gage_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gage-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> being distinguished from <i><a href="/wiki/Sternbergia" title="Sternbergia">Sternbergia</a></i> by the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Paraperigonium" class="mw-redirect" title="Paraperigonium">paraperigonium</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and is <a href="/wiki/Monophyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subdivision">Subdivision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Subdivision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The infrageneric phylogeny of <i>Narcissus</i> still remains relatively unsettled,<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> the taxonomy having proved complex and difficult to resolve,<sup id="cite_ref-FE5_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FE5-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Medrano_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medrano-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> due to the diversity of the wild species, the ease with which natural <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a> occurs, and extensive cultivation and breeding accompanied by escape and naturalisation.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, the number of accepted species has varied widely.<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>De Candolle, in the first systematic taxonomy of <i>Narcissus</i>, arranged the species into named groups, and those names have largely endured for the various subdivisions since and bear his name as their authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Candollea_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Candollea-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Candolleb_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Candolleb-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The situation was confused by the inclusion of many unknown or garden varieties, and it was not till the work of Baker that the wild species were all grouped as sections under one genus, <i>Narcissus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Baker_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A common classification system has been that of Fernandes <sup id="cite_ref-Fernandes68_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes68-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fernandes75_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes75-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fernandes51_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes51-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> based on <a href="/wiki/Cytology" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytology">cytology</a>, as modified by Blanchard (1990)<sup id="cite_ref-Blanchard_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blanchard-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levy_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Mathew" title="Brian Mathew">Mathew</a> (2002).<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Another is that of Meyer (1966).<sup id="cite_ref-Meyer_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Fernandes proposed two <a href="/wiki/Subgenera" class="mw-redirect" title="Subgenera">subgenera</a> based on basal chromosome number, and then subdivided these into ten <a href="/wiki/Section_(biology)" title="Section (biology)">sections</a> as did Blanchard.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other authors (e.g. Webb<sup id="cite_ref-FE5_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FE5-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebb1978_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebb1978-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>) prioritised morphology over genetics, abandoning subgenera, although Blanchard\'s system has been one of the most influential. While infrageneric groupings within <i>Narcissus</i> have been relatively constant, their status (genera, subgenera, sections, subsections, series, species) has not.<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The most cited system is that of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horticultural_Society" title="Royal Horticultural Society">Royal Horticultural Society</a> (RHS) which simply lists ten sections. Three of these are <a href="/wiki/Monotypic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotypic">monotypic</a> (contain only one species), while two others contain only two species. Most species are placed in section <i>Pseudonarcissus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-RHSBC_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSBC-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Santos-Gally_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santos-Gally-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Many of these subdivisions  correspond roughly to the popular names for daffodil types, <i>e.g.</i> Trumpet Daffodils, Tazettas, Pheasant\'s Eyes, Hoop Petticoats, Jonquils.<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical" class="mw-redirect" title="Hierarchical">hierarchical</a> system is that of Mathew, illustrated here -\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption><big>Table I: Subdivisions
