<small>L.</small>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogenetics">Phylogenetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Phylogenetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_analysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogenetic analysis">phylogenetic analysis</a> of Graham and Barrett (2004) supported the infrageneric division of <i>Narcissus</i> into two <a href="/wiki/Clades" class="mw-redirect" title="Clades">clades</a> corresponding to Fernandes\' subgenera, but did not support monophyly of all sections.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> A later extended analysis by <a href="/wiki/Nina_R%C3%B8nsted" title="Nina R\xc3\xb8nsted">R\xc3\xb8nsted</a> <i>et. al.</i> (2008) with additional taxa confirmed this pattern.<sup id="cite_ref-Ronsted2008_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ronsted2008-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A large molecular analysis by <a href="/wiki/Ben_Zonneveld" title="Ben Zonneveld">Zonneveld</a> (2008) sought to reduce some of the <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyly</a> identified by Graham and Barrett. This led to a revision of the sectional structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Santos-Gally_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santos-Gally-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FCasas2008_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCasas2008-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\nWhile Graham and Barrett (2004)<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> had determined that subgenus <i>Hermione</i> was monophyletic, Santos-Gally <i>et. al.</i> (2011)<sup id="cite_ref-Santos-Gally_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santos-Gally-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> did not. If two species excluded in the former study are removed from the analysis, the studies are in agreement, the species in question instead forming a clade with subgenus <i>Narcissus</i>. Some so-called nothosections have been proposed, to accommodate natural (\'ancient\') hybrids (nothospecies).<sup id="cite_ref-FCasas2008_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCasas2008-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Species">Species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Narcissus_species" title="List of Narcissus species">List of Narcissus species</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissi_Encylopaedia_Londinensis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Narcissi_Encylopaedia_Londinensis.jpg/220px-Narcissi_Encylopaedia_Londinensis.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Narcissi_Encylopaedia_Londinensis.jpg/330px-Narcissi_Encylopaedia_Londinensis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Narcissi_Encylopaedia_Londinensis.jpg/440px-Narcissi_Encylopaedia_Londinensis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="743" data-file-height="570" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissi_Encylopaedia_Londinensis.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_hispanicus" title="Narcissus hispanicus">N. major</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_triandrus" title="Narcissus triandrus">N. triandrus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_jonquilla" title="Narcissus jonquilla">N. jonquilla</a></i>. <i>Encyclopaedia Londinensis</i> 1819</div></div></div>\n<p>Estimates of the number of species in <i>Narcissus</i> have varied widely, from anywhere between 16 and almost 160,<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blanchard_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blanchard-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> even in the modern era. Linnaeus originally included six species in 1753, by 1784 there were fourteen<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELinn\xc3\xa91784&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid03gZAAAAYAAJvqnarcissuspgPA316vonepageqffalse_p.316&#93;_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinn\xc3\xa91784[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid03gZAAAAYAAJvqnarcissuspgPA316vonepageqffalse_p.316]-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> by 1819 sixteen,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkes1819_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkes1819-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> and by 1831 <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Hardy_Haworth" title="Adrian Hardy Haworth">Adrian Haworth</a> had described 150 species.<sup id="cite_ref-Haworth_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haworth-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Much of the variation lies in the definition of <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>. Thus, a very wide view of each species, such as Webb\'s<sup id="cite_ref-FE5_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FE5-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> results in few species, while a very narrow view such as that of Fernandes<sup id="cite_ref-Fernandes68_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes68-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> results in a larger number.<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Another factor is the status of <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a>, with a distinction between "ancient hybrids" and "recent hybrids". The term "ancient hybrid" refers to hybrids found growing over a large area, and therefore now considered as separate species, while "recent hybrid" refers to solitary plants found amongst their parents, with a more restricted range.<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld_53-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fernandes (1951) originally accepted 22 species,<sup id="cite_ref-Fernandes51_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes51-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Webb (1980) 27.<sup id="cite_ref-FE5_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FE5-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> By 1968, Fernandes had 63 species,<sup id="cite_ref-Fernandes68_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes68-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Blanchard (1990) 65 species,<sup id="cite_ref-Blanchard_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blanchard-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and Erhardt (1993) 66.<sup id="cite_ref-Erhardt_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erhardt-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In 2006 the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horticultural_Society" title="Royal Horticultural Society">Royal Horticultural Society</a>\'s (RHS) <i>International Daffodil Register and Classified List</i> <sup id="cite_ref-RHSBC_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSBC-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RHSIRA_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSIRA-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RHSIRAS_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSIRAS-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> listed 87 species, while Zonneveld\'s genetic study (2008) resulted in only 36.<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld_53-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> As of  September&#160;2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Checklist_of_Selected_Plant_Families" title="World Checklist of Selected Plant Families">World Checklist of Selected Plant Families</a> accepts 52 species, along with at least 60 hybrids,<sup id="cite_ref-WCSP_Narcissus_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCSP_Narcissus-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> while the RHS has 81 accepted names in its October 2014 list.<sup id="cite_ref-RHSbotnames_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSbotnames-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Within the <a href="/wiki/Narcisseae" title="Narcisseae">Narcisseae</a>, <i>Narcissus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Western_Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Mediterranean">western Mediterranean</a>) diverged from <i><a href="/wiki/Sternbergia" title="Sternbergia">Sternbergia</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>) some time in the <a href="/wiki/Late_Oligocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Oligocene">Late Oligocene</a> to <a href="/wiki/Early_Miocene" title="Early Miocene">Early Miocene</a> eras, around 29.3\xe2\x80\x9318.1 <a href="/wiki/Ma_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ma (unit)">Ma</a>. Later the genus divided into the two subgenera (<i>Hermione</i> and <i>Narcissus</i>) between 27.4\xe2\x80\x9316.1 Ma. The divisions between the sections of <i>Hermione</i> then took place during the Miocene period 19.9\xe2\x80\x937.8 Ma.<sup id="cite_ref-Santos-Gally_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santos-Gally-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <i>Narcissus</i> appears to have arisen in the area of the Iberian peninsula, southern France and north-western Italy. Subgenus <i>Hermione</i> in turn arose in the southwestern mediterranean and north west Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Santos-Gally_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santos-Gally-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Narcissus">Narcissus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Narcissus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clusius250.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Clusius250.jpg/220px-Clusius250.jpg" width="220" height="341" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Clusius250.jpg/330px-Clusius250.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Clusius250.jpg/440px-Clusius250.jpg 2x" data-file-width="634" data-file-height="984" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clusius250.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_jonquilla" title="Narcissus jonquilla">N. juncifolius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carolus_Clusius" title="Carolus Clusius">Carolus Clusius</a> </i>Rariorum stirpium<i> 1576</i></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:L%27Obel_Icones_Stirpium_112.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/L%27Obel_Icones_Stirpium_112.jpg/220px-L%27Obel_Icones_Stirpium_112.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/L%27Obel_Icones_Stirpium_112.jpg/330px-L%27Obel_Icones_Stirpium_112.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/L%27Obel_Icones_Stirpium_112.jpg/440px-L%27Obel_Icones_Stirpium_112.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1560" data-file-height="1240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:L%27Obel_Icones_Stirpium_112.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_poeticus" title="Narcissus poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Matthias_de_l%27Obel" title="Matthias de l&#39;Obel">Matthias de l\'Obel</a> <i>Icones stirpium</i> 1591</div></div></div>\n<p>The derivation of the Latin <i>narcissus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xbd\xce\xac\xcf\x81\xce\xba\xce\xb9\xcf\x83\xcf\x83\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</span>) is unknown, but may be connected with hell.<sup id="cite_ref-Prior_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prior-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> It is frequently linked to the myth of <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(mythology)" title="Narcissus (mythology)">Narcissus</a>, who became so obsessed with his own reflection in water that he drowned and the narcissus plant sprang from where he died. There is no evidence for the flower being named for the youth. <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_poeticus" title="Narcissus poeticus">Narcissus poeticus</a></i> which grows in Greece, has a fragrance that has been described as intoxicating.<sup id="cite_ref-SOED_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOED-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> wrote that the plant was named for its fragrance (<i>\xce\xbd\xce\xb1\xcf\x81\xce\xba\xce\xac\xcf\x89</i> narkao, "I grow numb" ), not the youth.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PlinyLat_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PlinyLat-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dweck_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dweck-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Groom1997_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groom1997-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, there were accounts of narcissi growing long before the story of Narcissus appeared (see <a href="#Greek_culture">Greek culture</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Prior_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prior-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pausanias_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pausanias-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;Note 2&#93;</a></sup> It has also been suggested that daffodils bending over streams represent the youth admiring his reflection.<sup id="cite_ref-Gardener_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gardener-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Linnaeus used the Latin name "narcissus" for the plant but was preceded by others such as <a href="/wiki/Matthias_de_l%27Obel" title="Matthias de l&#39;Obel">Matthias de l\'Obel</a> (1591)<sup id="cite_ref-Obel_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obel-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Carolus_Clusius" title="Carolus Clusius">Clusius</a> (1576).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClusius1576&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem30856page247mode1up_De_Narcisso,_ii&#93;,_pp._245-25&#93;_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClusius1576[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem30856page247mode1up_De_Narcisso,_ii],_pp._245-25]-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The name Narcissus was not uncommon for men in Roman times.\n</p><p>The plural form of the common name "narcissus" has caused some confusion. Dictionaries list "narcissi", "narcissuses" and "narcissus".<sup id="cite_ref-SOED_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOED-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Webster_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> However, texts on usage such as Garner<sup id="cite_ref-Garner_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garner-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and Fowler<sup id="cite_ref-Fowler_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fowler-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> state that "narcissi" is the preferred form. The common name narcissus should not be capitalised.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Daffodil">Daffodil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Daffodil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The name "daffodil" is derived from "affodell", a variant of <i><a href="/wiki/Asphodelus" title="Asphodelus">asphodel</a></i>. The narcissus was frequently referred to as the asphodel<sup id="cite_ref-Dweck_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dweck-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> (see <a href="#Antiquity">Antiquity</a>). Asphodel in turn appears to come from the Greek "asphodelos" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xe1\xbc\x80\xcf\x83\xcf\x86\xcf\x8c\xce\xb4\xce\xb5\xce\xbb\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-Dweck_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dweck-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The reason for the introduction of the initial "d" is not known.<sup id="cite_ref-Reece_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reece-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> From at least the 16th century, "daffadown dilly" and "daffydowndilly" have appeared as alternative names.<sup id="cite_ref-SOED_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOED-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Other names include "Lent lily".<sup id="cite_ref-Hereford_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hereford-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oxford_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Narcissus_species" title="List of Narcissus species">List of Narcissus species</a></div>\n<p>Although the <a href="/wiki/Amaryllidaceae" title="Amaryllidaceae">Amaryllidaceae</a> family are predominantly tropical or subtropical as a whole, <i>Narcissus</i> occurs primarily in <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean region">Mediterranean region</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Centre_of_diversity" class="mw-redirect" title="Centre of diversity">centre of diversity</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> (<a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> A few species extend the range into <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">southern France</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> (<i>N. poeticus</i>, <i>N. serotinus</i>, <i>N. tazetta</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a> (<i>N. serotinus</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> (<i>N. tazetta</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The occurrence of <i>N. tazetta</i> in western and central <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> are considered <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introductions</a>, albeit ancient<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> (see <a href="#Eastern_cultures">Eastern cultures</a>). While the exact northern limit of the natural range is unknown, the occurrences of wild <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, middle and northern Europe are similarly considered ancient introductions.<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAedo2010_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAedo2010-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEeMonocot2014_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEeMonocot2014-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Amaryllidaceae" title="Amaryllidaceae">Amaryllidaceae</a> is not native to North America, it grows well in USDA <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_zone" title="Hardiness zone">hardiness zones</a> 3B through 10, which encompass most of the United States and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_elegans" title="Narcissus elegans">N. elegans</a></i> occurs on the North West African Coast (<a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>), as well as the coastline of <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_bulbocodium" title="Narcissus bulbocodium">N. bulbocodium</a></i> between <a href="/wiki/Tangier" title="Tangier">Tangier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> and  Tangier to <a href="/wiki/Marrakech" class="mw-redirect" title="Marrakech">Marrakech</a>, but also on the Iberian Peninsula. <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_serotinus" title="Narcissus serotinus">N. serotinus</a></i> is found along the entire Mediterranean coast. <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_tazetta" title="Narcissus tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i> occurs as far east as <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>. Since this is one of the oldest species found in cultivation, it is likely to have been introduced into Kashmir. <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_poeticus" title="Narcissus poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_pseudonarcissus" title="Narcissus pseudonarcissus">N. pseudonarcissus</a></i> have the largest distribution ranges. <i>N. poeticus</i> ranges from the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Carpathians" title="Romanian Carpathians">Romanian Carpathians</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and along the <a href="/wiki/Dalmatian_coast" class="mw-redirect" title="Dalmatian coast">Dalmatian coast</a> to <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> ranges from the Iberian Peninsula, via the <a href="/wiki/Vosges_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Vosges Mountains">Vosges Mountains</a> to <a href="/wiki/Northern_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern France">northern France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> where there are still wild stocks in Southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>. The only occurrence in <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a> is located near Lellingen, in the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Kiischpelt" title="Kiischpelt">Kiischpelt</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> it is found mainly in the nature reserve at <a href="/wiki/Perlenbach-Fuhrtsbachtal" title="Perlenbach-Fuhrtsbachtal">Perlenbach-Fuhrtsbachtal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eifel_National_Park" title="Eifel National Park">Eifel National Park</a>, where in the spring at <a href="/wiki/Monschau" title="Monschau">Monschau</a> the meadows are teeming with yellow blooms.<sup id="cite_ref-PERLENBACH_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PERLENBACH-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> One of the most easterly occurrences can be found at <a href="/wiki/Misselberg" title="Misselberg">Misselberg</a> near <a href="/wiki/Nassau,_Rhineland-Palatinate" title="Nassau, Rhineland-Palatinate">Nassau</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Lahn" title="Lahn">Lahn</a>.  \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Narzissen_im_Perlenbachtal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Narzissen_im_Perlenbachtal.jpg/220px-Narzissen_im_Perlenbachtal.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Narzissen_im_Perlenbachtal.jpg/330px-Narzissen_im_Perlenbachtal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Narzissen_im_Perlenbachtal.jpg/440px-Narzissen_im_Perlenbachtal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Narzissen_im_Perlenbachtal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_pseudonarcissus" title="Narcissus pseudonarcissus">N. pseudonarcissus</a></i> growing in the <a href="/wiki/Perlenbach-Fuhrtsbachtal" title="Perlenbach-Fuhrtsbachtal">Perlenbach-Fuhrtsbachtal</a> reserve in Germany</div></div></div>\n<p>However unlike the above examples most species have very restricted <a href="/wiki/Endemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Endemic">endemic</a> ranges<sup id="cite_ref-Santos-Gally_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santos-Gally-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMedrano2008_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMedrano2008-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> which may overlap resulting in natural hybrids.<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld_53-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> For instance in the vicinity of the <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portuguese</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Porto" title="Porto">Porto</a> where both <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_triandrus" title="Narcissus triandrus">N. triandrus</a></i> occur there are found various intersections of the two species while in a small area along part of the Portuguese Montego river are found intersectional hybrids between <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_scaberulus" title="Narcissus scaberulus">N. scaberulus</a></i> and <i>N. triandrus</i>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeography</a> demonstrates a <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogenetic">phylogenetic</a> association, for instance subgenus <i>Hermione</i> having a lowland distribution, but subgenus <i>Narcissus</i> section <i>Apodanthi</i> being <a href="/wiki/Montane" class="mw-redirect" title="Montane">montane</a> and restricted to Morocco, Spain and Portugal. The remaining sections within subgenus <i>Narcissus</i> include both lowland and mountain habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-Santos-Gally_61-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santos-Gally-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Section <i>Pseudonarcissus</i>, although widely naturalised is endemic to the <a href="/wiki/Baetic_Ranges" class="mw-redirect" title="Baetic Ranges">Baetic Ranges</a> of the south eastern Iberian peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-Medrano_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medrano-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitats">Habitats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Habitats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Their native habitats are very varied, with different elevations, <a href="/wiki/Holdridge_life_zones" title="Holdridge life zones">bioclimatic areas</a> and substrates,<sup id="cite_ref-Santos-Gally_61-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santos-Gally-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> being found predominantly in open spaces ranging from low <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a> to rocky hillsides and <a href="/wiki/Montane_ecology" class="mw-redirect" title="Montane ecology">montane</a> <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pastures</a>, and including <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scrubland" class="mw-redirect" title="Scrubland">scrub</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bank_(geography)" title="Bank (geography)">river banks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fracture_(geology)" title="Fracture (geology)">rocky crevices</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Although requirements vary, overall there is a preference for <a href="/wiki/Soil_pH#Sources_of_Acidity" title="Soil pH">acidic</a> soils, although some species will grow on <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_scaberulus" title="Narcissus scaberulus">Narcissus scaberulus</a></i> will grow on <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> soils where it is moist in the growing season but dry in the summer, while <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_dubius" title="Narcissus dubius">Narcissus dubius</a></i> thrives best in regions with hot and dry summers.\n</p><p>The <i>Pseudonarcissus</i> group in their natural habitat prefer humid situations such as stream margins, springs, wet pastures, clearings of forests or shrublands with humid soils, and moist hillsides. These habitats tend to be discontinuous in the Mediterranean mountains, producing discrete isolated populations.<sup id="cite_ref-Medrano_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medrano-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, which has relatively little limestone, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_pseudonarcissus" title="Narcissus pseudonarcissus">Narcissus pseudonarcissus</a></i> grows in small groups on open mountain meadows or in mixed forests of <a href="/wiki/Fir" title="Fir">fir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraxinus" title="Fraxinus">ash</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birch</a> trees with well-drained soil.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Life cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Narcissus</i> are long-lived perennial geophytes with winter-growing and summer-dormant bulbs<sup id="cite_ref-Medrano_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medrano-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> that are mainly synanthous (leaves and flowers appearing at the same time).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> While most species flower in late winter to spring, five species are autumn flowering (<i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_broussonetii" title="Narcissus broussonetii">N. broussonetii</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_cavanillesii" title="Narcissus cavanillesii">N. cavanillesii</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_elegans" title="Narcissus elegans">N. elegans</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_serotinus" title="Narcissus serotinus">N. serotinus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_viridiflorus" title="Narcissus viridiflorus">N. viridiflorus</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>  By contrast these species are hysteranthous (leaves appear after flowering).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Flower longevity varies by species and conditions, ranging from 5\xe2\x80\x9320 days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA344_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae)&#93;_p._344_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA344_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae)]_p._344-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> After flowering leaf and root <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">senescence</a> sets in, and the plant appears to be \'dormant\' till the next spring, conserving moisture. However the dormant period is also one of considerable activity within the bulb <a href="/wiki/Primordia" class="mw-redirect" title="Primordia">primordia</a>. It is also a period during which the plant bulb may be susceptible to predators (see <a href="#Pests_and_diseases">Pests and diseases</a> below). Like many bulb plants from <a href="/wiki/Temperate" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate">temperate</a> regions, a period of exposure to cold is necessary before spring growth can begin. This protects the plant from growth during winter when intense cold may damage it. Warmer spring temperatures then initiate growth from the bulb. Early spring growth confers a number of advantages, including relative lack of competition for pollinators, and lack of <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> shading. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The exception to requiring cold temperatures to initiate flowering is <i><a href="/wiki/N._tazetta" class="mw-redirect" title="N. tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi&#93;,_p._104_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi],_p._104-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Plants may spread clonally through the production of daughter bulbs and division producing clumps.<sup id="cite_ref-Medrano_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medrano-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Narcissus species <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridise</a> readily, although the fertility of the offspring will depend on the parental relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollination">Pollination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Pollination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The flowers are <a href="/wiki/Insect_pollinated" class="mw-redirect" title="Insect pollinated">insect pollinated</a>, the major <a href="/wiki/Pollinators" class="mw-redirect" title="Pollinators">pollinators</a> being <a href="/wiki/Bees" class="mw-redirect" title="Bees">bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butterflies" class="mw-redirect" title="Butterflies">butterflies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flies" class="mw-redirect" title="Flies">flies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hawkmoths" class="mw-redirect" title="Hawkmoths">hawkmoths</a>, while the highly scented night flowering <i><a href="/wiki/N._viridiflorus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. viridiflorus">N. viridiflorus</a></i> is pollinated by <a href="/wiki/Crepuscular" class="mw-redirect" title="Crepuscular">crepuscular</a> moths. Pollination mechanism fall into three groups corresponding to floral morphology (see <a href="#Description">Description - Flowers</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA344_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae)&#93;,_p._344_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloydBarrett1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHgFA7mFHp2QCpgPA344_Barrett_S,_Lloyd_D._Stylar_polymorphisms_and_the_evolution_of_heterostyly_in_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_(Amaryllidaceae)],_p._344-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li>\'Daffodil\' form. Pollinated by bees seeking <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> from <a href="/wiki/Anthers" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthers">anthers</a> within the corona. The broad perianth allows bees (<i><a href="/wiki/Bombus" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombus">Bombus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Anthophora" title="Anthophora">Anthophora</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Andrena" title="Andrena">Andrena</a></i>) to completely enter the flower in their search for nectar and /or pollen. In this type the stigma lies in the mouth of the corona, extending beyond the six anthers, whose single <a href="/wiki/Whorl_(botany)" title="Whorl (botany)">whorl</a> lies well within the corona. The bees come into contact with the stigma before their legs, thorax and abdomen contact the anthers, and this <a href="/wiki/Approach_herkogamy" class="mw-redirect" title="Approach herkogamy">approach herkogamy</a> causes cross pollination.</li>\n<li>\'Paperwhite\' form. These are adapted to long-tongued <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Sphingid_moths" class="mw-redirect" title="Sphingid moths">sphingid moths</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Macroglossum" title="Macroglossum">Macroglossum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pieridae" title="Pieridae">Pieridae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nymphalidae" title="Nymphalidae">Nymphalidae</a> but also some long-tongued bees, and <a href="/wiki/Flies" class="mw-redirect" title="Flies">flies</a>, all of which are primarily seeking <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a>. The narrow tube admits only the insect\'s <a href="/wiki/Proboscis" title="Proboscis">proboscis</a>, while the short corona serves as a funnel guiding the tip of the proboscis into the mouth of the perianth tube. The stigma is placed either in the mouth of the tube, just above two whorls of three anthers, or hidden well below the anthers. The pollinators then carry pollen on their probosci or faces. The long tongued bees cannot reach the nectar at the tube base and so collect just pollen.</li>\n<li>\'Triandrus\' form. Pollinated by long-tongued solitary bees (<a href="/wiki/Anthophora" title="Anthophora">Anthophora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombus" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombus">Bombus</a>) which forage for both pollen and nectar. The large corona allows the bees to crawl into the perianth but then the narrow tube prevents further progres, causing them to probe deeply for nectar. The pendant flowers prevent pollination by lepidoptera. In <i>N. albimarginatus</i> there may be either a long stigma with short and mid length anthers or a short stigma and long anthers (<a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">dimorphism</a>). In <i>N. triandrus</i> there are three patterns of sexual organs (trimophism) but all have long upper anthers but vary in stigma position and the length of the lower anthers.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Allogamy" title="Allogamy">Allogamy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Outcrossing" title="Outcrossing">outcrossing</a>) on the whole is enforced through a late-acting (<a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">ovarian</a>) <a href="/wiki/Self-incompatibility" title="Self-incompatibility">self-incompatibility</a> system, but some species such as <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_dubius" title="Narcissus dubius">N. dubius</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_longispathus" title="Narcissus longispathus">N. longispathus</a></i> are self-compatible producing mixtures of selfed and <a href="/wiki/Outcrossed" class="mw-redirect" title="Outcrossed">outcrossed</a> seeds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDobson1997_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDobson1997-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_17-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests_and_diseases">Pests and diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Pests and diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Diseases of <i>Narcissus</i> are of concern because of the economic consequences of losses in commercial cultivation. Pests include viruses, bacteria, and fungi as well as arthropods and gastropods. For control of pests, see <a href="#Commercial_uses">Commercial uses</a>.\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Viruses" class="mw-redirect" title="Viruses">Viruses</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">Aphids</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Macrosiphum_euphorbiae" title="Macrosiphum euphorbiae">Macrosiphum euphorbiae</a></i> can transmit <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viral</a> diseases which affect the colour and shape of the leaves, as can <a href="/wiki/Nematodes" class="mw-redirect" title="Nematodes">Nematodes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZuckermanRohde1981&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpU9Mz--9wwoCpgPA286_Longidorus_transmitted_viruses&#93;,_p._286_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZuckermanRohde1981[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpU9Mz--9wwoCpgPA286_Longidorus_transmitted_viruses],_p._286-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Up to twenty five viruses have been described as being able to infect narcissi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPNW2014_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPNW2014-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerniak2013_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerniak2013-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsjes1996_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsjes1996-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> These include the <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_common_latent_virus" title="Narcissus common latent virus">Narcissus common latent virus</a></i> (NCLV, <i>Narcissus mottling-associated virus</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams2013&#91;httpwwwdpvwebnetnotessearchphp_&#39;&#39;Find_Viruses&#39;&#39;:_Narcissus&#93;_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams2013[httpwwwdpvwebnetnotessearchphp_&#39;&#39;Find_Viruses&#39;&#39;:_Narcissus]-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;Note 3&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_latent_virus" title="Narcissus latent virus">Narcissus latent virus</a></i> (NLV, <i>Narcissus mild mottle virus</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams2013&#91;httpwwwdpvwebnetnotessearchphp_&#39;&#39;Find_Viruses&#39;&#39;:_Narcissus&#93;_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams2013[httpwwwdpvwebnetnotessearchphp_&#39;&#39;Find_Viruses&#39;&#39;:_Narcissus]-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>) which causes green mottling near leaf tips,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1988&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid3gbPsC7i1IICpgPA3_Viruses&#93;,_p._3_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1988[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid3gbPsC7i1IICpgPA3_Viruses],_p._3-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams2013&#91;httpwwwdpvwebnetdpvshowdpvphpdpvno170_Brunt_AA._Narcissus_latent_virus._1976&#93;_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams2013[httpwwwdpvwebnetdpvshowdpvphpdpvno170_Brunt_AA._Narcissus_latent_virus._1976]-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_degeneration_virus" title="Narcissus degeneration virus">Narcissus degeneration virus</a></i> (NDV),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2007_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2007-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_late_season_yellows_virus" title="Narcissus late season yellows virus">Narcissus late season yellows virus</a></i> (NLSYV) which occurs after flowering, streaking the leaves and stems,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMowat1988_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMowat1988-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_mosaic_virus" title="Narcissus mosaic virus">Narcissus mosaic virus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_yellow_stripe_virus" title="Narcissus yellow stripe virus">Narcissus yellow stripe virus</a></i> (NYSV, <i>Narcissus yellow streak virus</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams2013&#91;httpwwwdpvwebnetnotessearchphp_&#39;&#39;Find_Viruses&#39;&#39;:_Narcissus&#93;_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams2013[httpwwwdpvwebnetnotessearchphp_&#39;&#39;Find_Viruses&#39;&#39;:_Narcissus]-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>), <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_tip_necrosis_virus" title="Narcissus tip necrosis virus">Narcissus tip necrosis virus</a></i> (NTNV) which produces necrosis of leaf tips after flowering<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams2013&#91;httpwwwdpvwebnetdpvshowdpvphpdpvno166_W._P._Mowat,_C._J._Asjes,_A._A._Brunt._Narcissus_tip_necrosis_virus._September_1976&#93;_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams2013[httpwwwdpvwebnetdpvshowdpvphpdpvno166_W._P._Mowat,_C._J._Asjes,_A._A._Brunt._Narcissus_tip_necrosis_virus._September_1976]-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_white_streak_virus" title="Narcissus white streak virus">Narcissus white streak virus</a></i> (NWSV).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Less host specific viruses include <i><a href="/wiki/Raspberry_ringspot_virus" title="Raspberry ringspot virus">Raspberry ringspot virus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nerine_latent_virus" title="Nerine latent virus">Nerine latent virus</a></i>  (NeLV) =<i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_symptomless_virus" title="Narcissus symptomless virus">Narcissus symptomless virus</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2006_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2006-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Arabis_mosaic_virus" title="Arabis mosaic virus">Arabis mosaic virus</a></i> (ArMV),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIwaki1974_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIwaki1974-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Fabavirus" title="Fabavirus">Broad Bean Wilt Viruses</a></i> (BBWV)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIwaki1972_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIwaki1972-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>  <i><a href="/wiki/Cucumber_mosaic_virus" title="Cucumber mosaic virus">Cucumber mosaic virus</a></i> (CMV), <i><a href="/wiki/Tomato_black_ring_virus" title="Tomato black ring virus">Tomato black ring virus</a></i> (TBRV), <i><a href="/wiki/Tomato_ringspot_virus" title="Tomato ringspot virus">Tomato ringspot virus</a></i> (TomRSV) and <i><a href="/wiki/Tobacco_rattle_virus" title="Tobacco rattle virus">Tobacco rattle virus</a></i> (TRV).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIwaki1972_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIwaki1972-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of these viruses the most serious and prevalent are NDV, NYSV and NWSV.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerniak2013_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerniak2013-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> NDV is associated with <a href="/wiki/Chlorotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Chlorotic">chlorotic</a> leaf striping in <i><a href="/wiki/N._tazetta" class="mw-redirect" title="N. tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2007_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2007-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Infection with NYSV produces light or grayish green, or yellow stripes or mottles on the upper two-thirds of the leaf, which may be roughened or twisted. The flowers which may be smaller than usual may also be streaked or blotched. NWSV produces greenish-purple streaking on the leaves and stem turning white to yellow, and premature senescence reducing bulb size and yield.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPNW2014_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPNW2014-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> These viruses are primarily diseases of commercial nurseries. The growth inhibition caused by viral infection can cause substantial economic damage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014&#91;httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases&#93;_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014[httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases]-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUCIPM2009_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUCIPM2009-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERHS2014&#91;httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilePID149_Daffodil_viruses&#93;_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERHS2014[httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilePID149_Daffodil_viruses]-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Bacterial" class="mw-redirect" title="Bacterial">Bacterial</a> disease is uncommon in <i>Narcissus</i> but includes <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas" title="Pseudomonas">Pseudomonas</a></i> (bacterial streak) and <i><a href="/wiki/Pectobacterium_carotovorum" title="Pectobacterium carotovorum">Pectobacterium carotovorum</a></i> sp. <i>carotovorum</i> (bacterial soft rot).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Fungi" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungi">Fungi</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p>More problematic for non-commercial plants is the <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fusarium_oxysporum" title="Fusarium oxysporum"><i>Fusarium oxysporum</i> f. sp. <i>narcissi</i></a>, which causes basal rot (rotting of the bulbs and yellowing of the leaves). This is the most serious disease of <i>Narcissus</i>. Since the fungus can remain in the soil for many years it is necessary to remove infected plants immediately, and to avoid planting further narcissi at that spot for a further five years. Not all species and cultivars are equally susceptible. Relatively resistant forms include <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_triandrus" title="Narcissus triandrus">N. triandrus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_tazetta" title="Narcissus tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_jonquilla" title="Narcissus jonquilla">N. jonquilla</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERHS2014&#91;httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilepid222_Narcissus_basal_rot&#93;_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERHS2014[httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilepid222_Narcissus_basal_rot]-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks1996_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks1996-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanksCarder2003_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanksCarder2003-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another fungus which attacks the bulbs, causing narcissus smoulder, is <i><a href="/wiki/Botrytis_narcissicola" title="Botrytis narcissicola">Botrytis narcissicola</a></i> (<i>Sclerotinia narcissicola</i>) and other species of <i><a href="/wiki/Botryotinia" title="Botryotinia">Botrytis</a></i>, including <i><a href="/wiki/Botrytis_cinerea" title="Botrytis cinerea">Botrytis cinerea</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;NeillMansfield1980_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;NeillMansfield1980-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHong2007_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHong2007-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> particularly if improperly stored. Copper sulfate is used to combat the disease, and infected bulbs are burned. Blue mould rot of bulbs may be caused by infection with species of <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium" title="Penicillium">Penicillium</a></i>, if they have become damaged either through mechanical injury or infestation by mites (see below).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPirone1978&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduz_JS112w_kCpgPA366_Narcissus&#93;,_p._366_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPirone1978[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduz_JS112w_kCpgPA366_Narcissus],_p._366-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Species of <a href="/wiki/Rhizopus" title="Rhizopus">Rhizopus</a> (<i>e.g.</i>  <i><a href="/wiki/Rhizopus_stolonifer" title="Rhizopus stolonifer">Rhizopus stolonifer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rhizopus_nigricans" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhizopus nigricans">Rhizopus nigricans</a></i>) cause bulb soft rot<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUCIPM2009_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUCIPM2009-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorst2013&#91;httpslinkspringercomchapter101007978-94-007-6049-3_17_Narcissus&#93;,_pp._95\xe2\x80\x9396_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorst2013[httpslinkspringercomchapter101007978-94-007-6049-3_17_Narcissus],_pp._95\xe2\x80\x9396-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sclerotinia_bulborum" title="Sclerotinia bulborum">Sclerotinia bulborum</a></i>, black slime disease.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoerema1989_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoerema1989-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> A combination of both <i><a href="/wiki/Peyronellaea_curtisii" title="Peyronellaea curtisii">Peyronellaea curtisii</a></i> (<i>Stagonosporopsis curtisii</i>) and <i>Botrytis narcissicola</i> causes neck rot in the bulbs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fungi affecting the roots include <i><a href="/wiki/Nectria_radicicola" title="Nectria radicicola">Nectria radicicola</a></i> (<i>Cylindrocarpon destructans</i>), a cause of root rot<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoerema1989_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoerema1989-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rosellinia_necatrix" title="Rosellinia necatrix">Rosellinia necatrix</a></i> causing white root rot,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMantell1973_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMantell1973-121">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> while others affect root and bulb, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus_niger" title="Aspergillus niger">Aspergillus niger</a></i> (black mold), and species of <i><a href="/wiki/Trichoderma" title="Trichoderma">Trichoderma</a></i>, including <i><a href="/wiki/Trichoderma_viride" title="Trichoderma viride">T. viride</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Trichoderma_harzianum" title="Trichoderma harzianum">T. harzianum</a></i> (=<i>T. narcissi</i>) responsible for green mold.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorst2013&#91;httpslinkspringercomchapter101007978-94-007-6049-3_17_Narcissus&#93;,_pp._95\xe2\x80\x9396_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorst2013[httpslinkspringercomchapter101007978-94-007-6049-3_17_Narcissus],_pp._95\xe2\x80\x9396-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other fungi affect the remainder of the plant. Another <i>Botrytis</i> fungus, <i><a href="/wiki/Botrytis_polyblastis" class="mw-redirect" title="Botrytis polyblastis">Botrytis polyblastis</a></i> (<i>Sclerotinia polyblastis</i>) causes brown spots on the flower buds and stems (narcissus fire), especially in damp weather and is a threat to the cut flower industry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore1959&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidI_M8AAAAIAAJpgPA343_&#39;&#39;Sclerotinia_polyblastis&#39;&#39;_p.343&#93;_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore1959[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidI_M8AAAAIAAJpgPA343_&#39;&#39;Sclerotinia_polyblastis&#39;&#39;_p.343]-122">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElad2007_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElad2007-123">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ramularia_vallisumbrosae" title="Ramularia vallisumbrosae">Ramularia vallisumbrosae</a></i> is a leaf spot fungus found in warmer climates, causing narcissus white mould disease.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory-124">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> <i>Peyronellaea curtisii</i>, the Narcissus leaf scorch, also affects the leaves<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014&#91;httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases&#93;_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014[httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases]-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUCIPM2009_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUCIPM2009-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vanLeeuwen_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanLeeuwen-125">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCain_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCain-126">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERHS2014&#91;httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilePID735_Narcissus_leaf_scorch&#93;_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERHS2014[httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilePID735_Narcissus_leaf_scorch]-127">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> as does its <a href="/wiki/Synanamorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Synanamorph">synanamorph</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Phoma_narcissi" title="Phoma narcissi">Phoma narcissi</a></i> (leaf tip blight).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrous2011_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrous2011-128">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Aecidium_narcissi" title="Aecidium narcissi">Aecidium narcissi</a></i> causes <a href="/wiki/Rust_(fungus)" title="Rust (fungus)">rust</a> lesions on leaves and stems.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoerema1989_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoerema1989-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Animals" class="mw-redirect" title="Animals">Animals</a>&#160;</dt>\n<dd></dd></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Arthropods" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthropods">Arthropods</a> that are <i>Narcissus</i> pests include <a href="/wiki/Insects" class="mw-redirect" title="Insects">insects</a> such as three species of <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">fly</a> that have larvae that attack the plants, narcissus bulb fly <i><a href="/wiki/Merodon_equestris" title="Merodon equestris">Merodon equestris</a></i>, and two species of <a href="/wiki/Hoverflies" class="mw-redirect" title="Hoverflies">hoverflies</a>, the lesser bulb flies <i><a href="/wiki/Eumerus_tuberculatus" title="Eumerus tuberculatus">Eumerus tuberculatus</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Creager_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creager-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Eumerus_strigatus" title="Eumerus strigatus">Eumerus strigatus</a></i>. The flies lay their eggs at the end of June in the ground around the narcissi, a single female fly being able to lay up to fifty eggs. The hatching <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a> then burrow through the soil towards the bulbs and consume their interiors. They then overwinter in the empty bulb shell, emerging in April to <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupate</a> in the soil, from which the adult fly emerges in May.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014&#91;httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases&#93;_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014[httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases]-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERHS2014&#91;httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilePID657_Narcissus_bulb_fly&#93;_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERHS2014[httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilePID657_Narcissus_bulb_fly]-130">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The larvae of some <a href="/wiki/Moths" class="mw-redirect" title="Moths">moths</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Korscheltellus_lupulina" class="mw-redirect" title="Korscheltellus lupulina">Korscheltellus lupulina</a></i> (the common swift moth) attack <i>Narcissus</i> bulbs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMAFF1970_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMAFF1970-131">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014&#91;httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases&#93;_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014[httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases]-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other arthropods include <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">Mites</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Steneotarsonemus_laticeps" title="Steneotarsonemus laticeps">Steneotarsonemus laticeps</a></i> (Bulb scale mite),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGratwick1992&#91;httpslinkspringercomchapter101007978-94-011-1490-5_66_Bulb_scale_mite&#93;,_pp._334\xe2\x80\x93337_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGratwick1992[httpslinkspringercomchapter101007978-94-011-1490-5_66_Bulb_scale_mite],_pp._334\xe2\x80\x93337-132">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rhizoglyphus" title="Rhizoglyphus">Rhizoglyphus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Histiostoma" title="Histiostoma">Histiostoma</a></i> infest mainly stored bulbs and multiply particularly at high ambient temperature, but do not attack planted bulbs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014&#91;httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases&#93;_110-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014[httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases]-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Planted bulbs are susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>, the most serious of which is <i><a href="/wiki/Ditylenchus_dipsaci" title="Ditylenchus dipsaci">Ditylenchus dipsaci</a></i> (Narcissus eelworm), the main cause of basal plate disease<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorSpiegel1993_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorSpiegel1993-133">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> in which the leaves turn yellow and become misshapen. Infested bulbs have to be destroyed; where infestation is heavy avoiding planting further narcissi for another five years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014&#91;httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases&#93;_110-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014[httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases]-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERHS2014&#91;httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilePID659_narcissus_eelworm&#93;_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERHS2014[httpswwwrhsorgukadviceprofilePID659_narcissus_eelworm]-134">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi&#93;,_p._107_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi],_p._107-135">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERavichandra2014&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidOfS_AwAAQBAJpgPA376_Stem_and_bulb_nematode_(&#39;&#39;Ditylenchus_dipsaci&#39;&#39;)&#93;,_pp._11,_94,_376_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERavichandra2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidOfS_AwAAQBAJpgPA376_Stem_and_bulb_nematode_(&#39;&#39;Ditylenchus_dipsaci&#39;&#39;)],_pp._11,_94,_376-136">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Other nematodes include <i><a href="/wiki/Aphelenchoides_subtenuis" title="Aphelenchoides subtenuis">Aphelenchoides subtenuis</a></i>, which penetrates the roots causing basal plate disease<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorSpiegel1993_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorSpiegel1993-133">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2013_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2013-137">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pratylenchus_penetrans" title="Pratylenchus penetrans">Pratylenchus penetrans</a></i> (lesion nematode) the main cause of root rot in narcissi.\n<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlootweg1956_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlootweg1956-138">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Other nematodes such as the <a href="/wiki/Longodorids" class="mw-redirect" title="Longodorids">longodorids</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Longidorus" title="Longidorus">Longidorus</a></i> spp. or needle nematodes and <i><a href="/wiki/Xiphinema" title="Xiphinema">Xiphinema</a></i> spp. or dagger nematodes) and the stubby-root nematodes or <a href="/wiki/Trichodorids" class="mw-redirect" title="Trichodorids">trichodorids</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Paratrichodorus" title="Paratrichodorus">Paratrichodorus</a></i> spp. and <i><a href="/wiki/Trichodorus" title="Trichodorus">Trichodorus</a></i> spp.) can also act as vectors of virus diseases, such as TBRV and TomRSV, in addition to causing stunting of the roots.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZuckermanRohde1981&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpU9Mz--9wwoCpgPA286_Longidorus_transmitted_viruses&#93;,_p._286_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZuckermanRohde1981[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidpU9Mz--9wwoCpgPA286_Longidorus_transmitted_viruses],_p._286-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2013_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2013-137">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gastropods" class="mw-redirect" title="Gastropods">Gastropods</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slug" title="Slug">slugs</a> also cause damage to growth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014&#91;httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases&#93;_110-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaffodil_Society2014[httpthedaffodilsocietycomwordpressa-guide-to-dafodilspests-diseases_Pests_&amp;_Diseases]-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUCIPM2009_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUCIPM2009-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Many of the smallest species have become extinct, requiring vigilance in the conservation of the wild species.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1_\xe2\x80\x93_29_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1_\xe2\x80\x93_29-139">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dweck_73-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dweck-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hanks_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hanks-140">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Narcissi are increasingly under threat by over-collection and threats to their natural habitats by urban development and tourism. <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_cyclamineus" title="Narcissus cyclamineus">N. cyclamineus</a></i> has been considered to be either extinct or exceedingly rare<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> but is not currently considered endangered, and is protected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIUCN2014&#91;httpwwwiucnredlistorgdetails1618990_IUCN_&#39;&#39;Narcissus_cyclamineus&#39;&#39;&#93;_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIUCN2014[httpwwwiucnredlistorgdetails1618990_IUCN_&#39;&#39;Narcissus_cyclamineus&#39;&#39;]-141">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> <a href="/wiki/Red_List" class="mw-redirect" title="Red List">Red List</a> describes five species as \'<a href="/wiki/Endangered" class="mw-redirect" title="Endangered">Endangered</a>\' (<i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_alcaracensis" title="Narcissus alcaracensis">Narcissus alcaracensis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_bujei" title="Narcissus bujei">Narcissus bujei</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_longispathus" title="Narcissus longispathus">Narcissus longispathus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_nevadensis" title="Narcissus nevadensis">Narcissus nevadensis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_radinganorum" class="mw-redirect" title="Narcissus radinganorum">Narcissus radinganorum</a></i>). In 1999 three species were considered endangered, five as vulnerable and six as rare.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In response a number of species have been granted protected species status and protected areas (meadows) have been established such as the <a href="/wiki/Negra%C5%9Fi_Daffodil_Meadow" class="mw-redirect" title="Negra\xc5\x9fi Daffodil Meadow">Negra\xc5\x9fi Daffodil Meadow</a> in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Kempley_Daffodil_Meadow" title="Kempley Daffodil Meadow">Kempley Daffodil Meadow</a> in the <a href="/wiki/UK" class="mw-redirect" title="UK">UK</a>. These areas often host <a href="#Festivals">daffodil festivals</a> in the spring.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cultivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><i>Magna cura non indigent Narcissi</i><br />Most easy of cultivation is the Narcissus</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Peter_Lauremberg" title="Peter Lauremberg">Peter Lauremberg</a>, <i>Apparatus plantarius: de plantis bulbosis et de plantis tuberosis</i> 1632<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELauremberg1632&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJjRAAAAAcAAJpgPA96_Lib._I_De_Plantis_bulbosis._cap._XVIII_Narcissus._V._Cultura&#93;,_p._96_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELauremberg1632[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJjRAAAAAcAAJpgPA96_Lib._I_De_Plantis_bulbosis._cap._XVIII_Narcissus._V._Cultura],_p._96-142">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_illustrations">Early illustrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Early illustrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a Gerard</a>, <i>The Herball</i> 1597\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_poeticus" title="Narcissus poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hale_(agriculturist)" title="Thomas Hale (agriculturist)">Thomas Hale</a>, <i>Eden: Or, a Compleat Body of Gardening</i> 1757\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: data-file-width="281" data-file-height="534" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Narcissus, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lauremberg" title="Peter Lauremberg">Peter Lauremberg</a> 1632\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:ParkinsonNarcissus101.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/ParkinsonNarcissus101.jpg/70px-ParkinsonNarcissus101.jpg" width="70" class="gallerytext">\n<p>Narcissi, <a href="/wiki/John_Parkinson_(botanist)" title="John Parkinson (botanist)">John Parkinson</a>, <i>Paradisus Terrestris</i> 1629. (8. Great Double Yellow Spanish Daffodil)\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<p>Of all the flowering plants, the bulbous have been the most popular for cultivation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrelage1890_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrelage1890-143">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Of these, narcissi are one of the most important spring flowering bulb plants in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA103_Narcissi&#93;,_p._103_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA103_Narcissi],_p._103-144">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus&#93;,_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKubitzki1998[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFyPVYzL76sMCpgPA100_Narcissus],_pp._100\xe2\x80\x93101-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_(ecology)" title="Indigenous (ecology)">Indigenous</a> in Europe, the wild populations of the parent species had been known since <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">antiquity</a>. Narcissi have been cultivated from at least as early as the sixteenth century in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, when large numbers of bulbs where imported from
