the field, particularly <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_hispanicus" title="Narcissus hispanicus">Narcissus hispanicus</a></i>, which soon became nearly extinct in its native habitat of France and Spain, though still found in the southern part of that country.<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld11_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld11-145">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The only large-scale production at that time related to the double narcissus "Van Sion" and cultivars of <i><a href="/wiki/N._tazetta" class="mw-redirect" title="N. tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i> imported in 1557.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoorenbos1954_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoorenbos1954-146">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cultivation is also documented in Britain at this time,<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-147">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddqxBAAAAIAAJpgPA7vonepageqffalse_p._7&#93;_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddqxBAAAAIAAJpgPA7vonepageqffalse_p._7]-148">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nesbitt2005_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nesbitt2005-149">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> although contemporary accounts show it was well known as a favourite garden and wild flower long before that and was used in making <a href="/wiki/Garland" title="Garland">garlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA73_Daffodils&#93;,_p._73_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA73_Daffodils],_p._73-150">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> This was a period when the development of exotic formal gardens and parks was becoming popular, particularly in what is known as the "Oriental Period" (1560\xe2\x80\x931620). In his <i>Hortus Medicus</i> (1588), the first catalogue of a German garden\'s plants,<sup id="cite_ref-Paeonia_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paeonia-151">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joachim_Camerarius_the_Younger" title="Joachim Camerarius the Younger">Joachim Camerarius the Younger</a> states that nine different types of daffodils were represented in his garden in <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Camerarius_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Camerarius-152">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>  After his death in 1598, his plants were moved by <a href="/wiki/Basilius_Besler" title="Basilius Besler">Basilius Besler</a> to the gardens they had designed at <a href="/wiki/Willibaldsburg" title="Willibaldsburg">Willibaldsburg</a>, the bishop\'s palace at <a href="/wiki/Eichst%C3%A4tt" title="Eichst\xc3\xa4tt">Eichst\xc3\xa4tt</a>, Upper Bavaria. That garden is described in Besler\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Hortus_Eystettensis" title="Hortus Eystettensis">Hortus Eystettensis</a></i> (1613) by which time there were 43 different types present.<sup id="cite_ref-Besler_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Besler-153">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Another German source at this time was <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lauremberg" title="Peter Lauremberg">Peter Lauremberg</a> who gives an account of the species known to him and their cultivation in his <i>Apparatus plantarius: de plantis bulbosis et de plantis tuberosis</i> (1632).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELauremberg1632_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELauremberg1632-154">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>\'s daffodil is the wild or true English daffodil (<i><a href="/wiki/N._pseudonarcissus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. pseudonarcissus">N. pseudonarcissus</a></i>),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA73_Daffodils&#93;,_p._73_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA73_Daffodils],_p._73-150">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> many other species were introduced, some of which escaped and naturalised, particularly <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_biflorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Narcissus biflorus">N. biflorus</a></i> (a hybrid) in <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> and the west of England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddqxBAAAAIAAJpgPA8vonepageqffalse_Preface&#93;,_p._8_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddqxBAAAAIAAJpgPA8vonepageqffalse_Preface],_p._8-155">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Gerard" title="John Gerard">Gerard</a>, in his extensive discussion of daffodils, both wild and cultivated ("bastard daffodils") described twenty four species in London gardens (1597),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddqxBAAAAIAAJpgPA8vonepageqffalse_Preface&#93;,_p._8_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddqxBAAAAIAAJpgPA8vonepageqffalse_Preface],_p._8-155">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGerard1597&#91;httpsarchiveorgstreammobot31753000817749page108mode2up_i._caps_75\xe2\x80\x9376,_pp._108\xe2\x80\x93116&#93;_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGerard1597[httpsarchiveorgstreammobot31753000817749page108mode2up_i._caps_75\xe2\x80\x9376,_pp._108\xe2\x80\x93116]-156">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUVA2007_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUVA2007-157">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> ("we have them all and every one of them in our London gardens, in great abundance", p.&#160;114).\n</p><p>In the early seventeenth century, <a href="/wiki/John_Parkinson_(botanist)" title="John Parkinson (botanist)">Parkinson</a> helped to ensure the popularity of the daffodil as a cultivated plant<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddqxBAAAAIAAJpgPA8vonepageqffalse_Preface_p._8&#93;_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddqxBAAAAIAAJpgPA8vonepageqffalse_Preface_p._8]-158">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> by describing a hundred different varieties in his <i>Paradisus Terrestris</i> (1629),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParkinson1629&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem30647page77mode1up_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;._The_Daffodil._pp.&amp;nbsp;67\xe2\x80\x93108&#93;_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParkinson1629[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem30647page77mode1up_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;._The_Daffodil._pp.&amp;nbsp;67\xe2\x80\x93108]-159">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> and introducing the great double yellow Spanish daffodil (<i>Pseudonarcissus aureus Hispanicus flore pleno</i> or Parkinson\'s Daffodil, see illustration) to England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParkinson1629&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem30647page113mode1up_p.&amp;nbsp;103&#93;_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParkinson1629[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem30647page113mode1up_p.&amp;nbsp;103]-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I thinke none ever had this kind before myselfe nor did I myself ever see it before the year 1618 for it is of mine own raising and flowering first in my own garden</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/John_Parkinson_(botanist)" title="John Parkinson (botanist)">John Parkinson</a>, <i>Paradisus Terrestris</i> 1632<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParkinson1629&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem30647page113mode1up_p.&amp;nbsp;103&#93;_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParkinson1629[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem30647page113mode1up_p.&amp;nbsp;103]-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Although not achieving the sensationalism of <a href="/wiki/Tulips" class="mw-redirect" title="Tulips">tulips</a>, daffodils and narcissi have been much celebrated in art and literature (see <a href="#The_Arts">The Arts</a>). The largest demand for narcissi bulbs were large trumpet daffodils, <i>N. poeticus</i> and <i>N. bulbocodium</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> became important in the shipping of bulbs to western Europe. By the early <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">baroque</a> period both tulips and narcissi were an important component of the spring garden. By 1739 a Dutch nursery catalogue listed 50 different varieties. A catalog of a Dutch nursery from 1739 already counted 50 varieties. In 1757 <a href="/wiki/John_Hill_(botanist)" title="John Hill (botanist)">Hill</a> gave an account of the history and cultivation of the daffodil in his edited version of the works of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hale_(agriculturist)" title="Thomas Hale (agriculturist)">Thomas Hale</a>, writing "The garden does not afford, in its Kind, a prettier plant than this; nor do we know one that has been so early, or so honorably mention\'d by all Kinds of Writers" (see illustration).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHale1757&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem131789page585mode1up_pp._495-496&#93;_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHale1757[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem131789page585mode1up_pp._495-496]-161">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Interest grew further when varieties that could be grown indoors became available, primarily the bunch flowered (multiple flower heads) <i>N. tazetta</i> (Polyanthus Narcissus).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrelage1890_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrelage1890-143">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> However interest varied by country. Maddock (1792) does not include narcissi in his list of the eight most important cultivated flowering plants in England,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaddock1792_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaddock1792-162">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> whereas in the Netherlands van Kampen (1760) stated that <i>N. tazetta</i> (<i>Narcisse \xc3\xa0 bouquet</i>) is the fifth most important \xe2\x80\x93 "<i>Le Narcisse \xc3\xa0 bouquet est la premiere fleur, apr\xc3\xa8s les Jacinthes, les Tulipes les Renoncules, et les Anemones, (dont nous avons d\xc3\xa9ja parl\xc3\xa9,) qui merite n\xc3\xb4tre attention</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Kampen_et_fils1760&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid-3ZYAAAAcAAJpgPA96_p._96&#93;_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Kampen_et_fils1760[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid-3ZYAAAAcAAJpgPA96_p._96]-163">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Kampen_&amp;_Son1764&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDIYmAQAAMAAJpgPA80_p._80&#93;_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Kampen_&amp;_Son1764[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDIYmAQAAMAAJpgPA80_p._80]-164">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Similarly <a href="/wiki/Philip_Miller" title="Philip Miller">Philip Miller</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Gardeners_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Gardeners Dictionary">Gardeners Dictionary</a></i> (1731\xe2\x80\x931768) refers to cultivation in Holland, Flanders and France, but not England,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1735&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem71189page177mode1up_vol._ii_Narcissus&#93;_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1735[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem71189page177mode1up_vol._ii_Narcissus]-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> because it was too difficult, a similar observation was made by <a href="/wiki/Sir_James_Justice" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir James Justice">Sir James Justice</a> at this time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJustice1771&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem69306page366mode1up_The_Gardiner&#39;s_new_Director_p._304&#93;_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJustice1771[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem69306page366mode1up_The_Gardiner&#39;s_new_Director_p._304]-166">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> However, for most species of <i>Narcissus</i> Lauremberg\'s <a href="/wiki/Dictum" title="Dictum">dictum</a> <i>Magna cura non indigent Narcissi</i> was much cited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA76_Daffodils&#93;,_p._76_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA76_Daffodils],_p._76-167">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Narcissi became an important <a href="/wiki/Horticultural" class="mw-redirect" title="Horticultural">horticultural</a> crop in Western Europe in the latter part of the nineteenth century, beginning in England between 1835 and 1855 and the end of the century in the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi&#93;,_p._104_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi],_p._104-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> By the beginning of the twentieth century 50 million bulbs of <i>N. Tazetta</i> "Paperwhite" were being exported annually from the Netherlands to the United States. With the production of <a href="/wiki/Polyploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyploid">triploids</a> such as "Golden Spur", in the late nineteenth century, and in the beginning of the twentieth century, tetraploids like "King Alfred" (1899), the industry was well established, with trumpet daffodils dominating the market.<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld11_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld11-145">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horticultural_Society" title="Royal Horticultural Society">Royal Horticultural Society</a> has been an important factor in promoting narcissi, holding the first Daffodil Conference in 1884,<sup id="cite_ref-DaffConf_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DaffConf-168">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Daffodil_Society" title="Daffodil Society">Daffodil Society</a>, the first organisation dedicated to the cultivation of narcissi was founded in Birmingham in 1898. Other countries followed and the <a href="/wiki/American_Daffodil_Society" title="American Daffodil Society">American Daffodil Society</a> which was founded in 1954 publishes <i>The Daffodil Journal</i> quarterly, a leading trade publication.\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:134px;max-width:134px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Daffodil trumpets</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus-closeup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Narcissus-closeup.jpg/130px-Narcissus-closeup.jpg" width="130" height="98" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Narcissus-closeup.jpg/195px-Narcissus-closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Narcissus-closeup.jpg/260px-Narcissus-closeup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Daffodil_plant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Daffodil_plant.jpg/130px-Daffodil_plant.jpg" width="130" height="173" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Daffodil_plant.jpg/195px-Daffodil_plant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Daffodil_plant.jpg/260px-Daffodil_plant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1280" /></a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>Narcissi are now popular as <a href="/wiki/Ornamental_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Ornamental plants">ornamental plants</a> for gardens, parks and as <a href="/wiki/Cut_flowers" title="Cut flowers">cut flowers</a>, providing colour from the end of winter to the beginning of summer in <a href="/wiki/Temperateness" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperateness">temperate</a> regions. They are one of the most popular spring flowers<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuxleyGriffithsLevy1992_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuxleyGriffithsLevy1992-169">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> and one of the major ornamental spring flowering bulb crops, being produced both for their bulbs and cut flowers, though cultivation of private and public spaces is greater than the area of commercial production.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Over a century of breeding has resulted in thousands of varieties and <a href="/wiki/Cultivars" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultivars">cultivars</a> being available from both general and specialist suppliers.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> They are normally sold as dry bulbs to be planted in late summer and autumn. They are one of the most economically important ornamental plants.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_13-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeders" class="mw-redirect" title="Plant breeders">Plant breeders</a> have developed some daffodils with double, triple, or ambiguously multiple rows and layers of segments.<sup id="cite_ref-RHSAZ_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSAZ-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Many of the breeding programs have concentrated on the corona (trumpet or cup), in terms of its length, shape, and colour, and the surrounding perianth<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> or even as in varieties derived from <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_poeticus" title="Narcissus poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i> a very reduced form.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_gardens">In gardens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: In gardens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>While some wild narcissi are specific in terms of their ecological requirements, most garden varieties are relatively tolerant of soil conditions,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> however very wet soils and clay soils may benefit from the addition of sand to improve drainage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrinklein2007_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrinklein2007-171">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The optimum soil is a neutral to slightly acid <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of 6.5\xe2\x80\x937.0.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bulbs offered for sale are referred to as either \'round\' or \'double nose\'. Round bulbs are circular in cross section and produce a single flower stem, while double nose bulbs have more than one bulb stem attached at the base and produce two or more flower stems, but bulbs with more than two stems are unusual.<sup id="cite_ref-CNLA_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNLA-172">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Planted narcissi bulbs produce daughter bulbs in the axil of the bulb scales, leading to the dying off of the exterior scales.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> To prevent planted bulbs forming more and more small bulbs, they can be dug up every 5\xe2\x80\x937 years, and the daughters separated and replanted separately, provided that a piece of the basal plate, where the rootlets are formed, is preserved. For daffodils to flower at the end of the winter or early spring, bulbs are planted in <a href="/wiki/Autumn" title="Autumn">autumn</a> (September\xe2\x80\x93November). This plant does well in ordinary soil but flourishes best in rich soil. Daffodils like the sun but also accepts partial shade exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Narcissi are well suited for planting under small thickets of trees, where they can be grouped as 6\xe2\x80\x9312 bulbs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidBfb1T6QbfaACpg251_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_p.&amp;nbsp;251&#93;_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidBfb1T6QbfaACpg251_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_p.&amp;nbsp;251]-174">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> They also grow well in perennial borders,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001_170-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> especially in association with <a href="/wiki/Day_lilies" class="mw-redirect" title="Day lilies">day lilies</a> which begin to form their leaves as the narcissi flowers are fading.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrinklein2007_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrinklein2007-171">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>  A number of wild species and hybrids such as "Dutch Master", "Golden Harvest", "Carlton", "Kings Court" and "Yellow Sun" naturalise well in lawns,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001_170-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> but it is important not to mow the lawn till the leaves start to fade, since they are essential for nourishing the bulb for the next flowering season.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001_170-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanaLerner2001-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Blue <a href="/wiki/Scilla" title="Scilla">Scilla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muscari" title="Muscari">Muscari</a> which also naturalise well in lawns and flower at the same time as narcissus, make an attractive contrast to the yellow flowers of the latter. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Tulips" class="mw-redirect" title="Tulips">Tulips</a>, narcissi bulbs are not attractive to rodents and are sometimes planted near tree roots in orchards to protect them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteinbergs2008_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteinbergs2008-175">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propagation">Propagation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Propagation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The commonest form of commercial propagation is by <a href="/wiki/Twin-scaling" title="Twin-scaling">twin-scaling</a>, in which the bulbs are cut into many small pieces but with two scales still connected by a small fragment of the basal plate. The fragments are disinfected and placed on nutrient media. Some 25\xe2\x80\x9335 new plants can be produced from a single bulb after four years. <a href="/wiki/Micropropagation" title="Micropropagation">Micropropagation</a> methods are not used for commercial production but is used for establishing commercial stock.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChow1993_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChow1993-176">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi&#93;,_p._107_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi],_p._107-135">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For commercial use, varieties with a minimum stem length of 30&#160;cm are sought, making them ideal for cut flowers. Florists require blooms that only open when they reach the retail outlet. For garden plants the objectives are to continually expand the colour palette and to produce hardy forms, and there is a particular demand for miniature varieties. The cultivars so produced tend to be larger and more robust than the wild types.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The main species used in breeding are <i><a href="/wiki/N._bulbocodium" class="mw-redirect" title="N. bulbocodium">N. bulbocodium</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/N._cyclamineus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. cyclamineus">N. cyclamineus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/N._jonquilla" class="mw-redirect" title="N. jonquilla">N. jonquilla</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/N._poeticus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/N._pseudonarcissus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. pseudonarcissus">N. pseudonarcissus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/N._serotinus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. serotinus">N. serotinus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/N._tazetta" class="mw-redirect" title="N. tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA105_Narcissi&#93;,_p._105_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA105_Narcissi],_p._105-177">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> gave rise to trumpet <a href="/wiki/Cultivars" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultivars">cultivars</a> with coloured tepals and corona, while its subspecies <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> subsp. <i>bicolor</i> was used for white tepaled varieties. To produce large cupped varieties, <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> was <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">crossed</a> with <i>N. poeticus</i>, and to produce small cupped varieties back crossed with <i>N. poeticus</i>.  Multiheaded varieties, often called "Poetaz" are mainly hybrids of <i>N. poeticus</i> and <i>N. tazetta</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_-_Cultivars.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Narcissus_-_Cultivars.jpg/220px-Narcissus_-_Cultivars.jpg" width="220" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Narcissus_-_Cultivars.jpg/330px-Narcissus_-_Cultivars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Narcissus_-_Cultivars.jpg/440px-Narcissus_-_Cultivars.jpg 2x" data-file-width="788" data-file-height="775" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_-_Cultivars.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Range of <i>Narcissus</i> <a href="/wiki/Cultivars" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultivars">cultivars</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Narcissus_horticultural_divisions" title="List of Narcissus horticultural divisions">List of Narcissus horticultural divisions</a></div>\n<p>For horticultural purposes, all <i>Narcissus</i> cultivars are split into 13 divisions as first described by Kington (1998),<sup id="cite_ref-Kington_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kington-178">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> for <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Horticultural_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="The Royal Horticultural Society">the Royal Horticultural Society</a> (RHS),<sup id="cite_ref-RHSAZ_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSAZ-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> based partly upon flower form (shape and length of corona), number of flowers per stem, flowering period and partly upon genetic background. Division 13, which includes wild daffodils, is the exception to this scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-RHSClass_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSClass-179">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The classification is a useful tool for planning planting. Most commercially available narcissi come from Divisions 1 (Trumpet), 2 (Large cupped) and 8 (Tazetta).\n</p><p>Growers register new daffodil cultivars by name and color with the Royal Horticultural Society, which is the international registration authority for the genus.<sup id="cite_ref-RHSIRA_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSIRA-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Their <i>International Daffodil Register</i> is regularly updated with supplements available online<sup id="cite_ref-RHSIRA_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSIRA-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and is searchable.<sup id="cite_ref-Mathew_22-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RHSIRAS_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHSIRAS-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The most recent supplement (2014) is the sixth (the fifth was published in 2012).<sup id="cite_ref-Kington2008_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kington2008-180">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> More than 27,000 names were registered as of 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-Kington2008_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kington2008-180">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> and the number has continued to grow. Registered daffodils are given a division number and <a href="#Colour_code">colour code</a><sup id="cite_ref-IDRI_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IDRI-181">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> such as 5 W-W ("Thalia").<sup id="cite_ref-BBThalia_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBThalia-182">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> In horticultural usage it is common to also find an unofficial Division 14: Miniatures, which although drawn from the other 13 divisions, have their miniature size in common.<sup id="cite_ref-BB14_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BB14-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Over 140 varieties have gained the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horticultural_Society" title="Royal Horticultural Society">Royal Horticultural Society</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Award_of_Garden_Merit" title="Award of Garden Merit">Award of Garden Merit</a> (See <a href="/wiki/List_of_Award_of_Garden_Merit_narcissus" title="List of Award of Garden Merit narcissus">List of Award of Garden Merit narcissus</a>).\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colour_code">Colour code</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Colour code">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_Geranium.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Narcissus_Geranium.jpg/220px-Narcissus_Geranium.jpg" width="220" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Narcissus_Geranium.jpg/330px-Narcissus_Geranium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Narcissus_Geranium.jpg/440px-Narcissus_Geranium.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1622" data-file-height="1457" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_Geranium.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Narcissus</i> "Geranium" <b>8 W-O</b></div></div></div>\n<p>Daffodil breeding has introduced a wide range of colours, in both the outer perianth tepal segment and the inner corona. In the registry, daffodils are coded by the colours of each of these two parts. Thus "Geranium", Tazetta (Division 8) as illustrated here with a white outer perianth and orange corona is classified as <b>8 W-O</b>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacology">Pharmacology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Pharmacology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>All <i>Narcissus</i> species contain the <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloid</a> poison <a href="/wiki/Lycorine" title="Lycorine">lycorine</a>, mostly in the bulb but also in the leaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Pupils_ill_after_bulb_put_in_soup_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pupils_ill_after_bulb_put_in_soup-184">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Members of the monocot subfamily <a href="/wiki/Amaryllidoideae" title="Amaryllidoideae">Amaryllidoideae</a> present a unique type of alkaloids, the norbelladine alkaloids, which are <a href="/wiki/4-methylcatechol" class="mw-redirect" title="4-methylcatechol">4-methylcatechol</a> derivatives combined with <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">tyrosine</a>. They are responsible for the poisonous properties of a number of the species. Over 200 different chemical structures of these compounds are known, of  which 79 or more are known from <i>Narcissus</i> alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin-185">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The toxic effects of ingesting <i>Narcissus</i> products for both humans and animals (such as cattle, goats, pigs and cats) have long been recognised and they have been used in suicide attempts. Ingestion of <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> or <i>N. jonquilla</i> is followed by <a href="/wiki/Salivation" class="mw-redirect" title="Salivation">salivation</a>, acute abdominal pains, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, then neurological and cardiac events, including <a href="/wiki/Trembling" class="mw-redirect" title="Trembling">trembling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Convulsions" class="mw-redirect" title="Convulsions">convulsions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a>. Death may result if large quantities are consumed.\n</p><p>The toxicity of <i>Narcissus</i> varies with species, <i>N. poeticus</i> being more toxic than <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i>, for instance. The distribution of toxins within the plant also varies, for instance there is a five times higher concentration of alkaloid in the stem of <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_papyraceus" title="Narcissus papyraceus">N. papyraceus</a></i> than in the bulb, making it dangerous to herbivores more likely to consume the stem than the bulb, and is part of the plant\'s defence mechanisms. The distribution of alkaloids within tissues may also reflect defence against parasites.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The bulbs can also be toxic to other nearby plants, including roses, rice, and cabbages, inhibiting growth.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> For instance placing cut flowers in a vase alongside other flowers shortens the life of the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-Kornienko_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kornienko-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poisoning">Poisoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Poisoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Many cases of poisoning or death have occurred when narcissi bulbs have been mistaken for <a href="/wiki/Leek" title="Leek">leeks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Onion" title="Onion">onions</a> and cooked and eaten. Recovery is usually complete in a few hours without any specific intervention. In more severe cases involving ingestion of large quantities of bulbs, <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a>, salts and <a href="/wiki/Laxatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Laxatives">laxatives</a> may be required, and for severe symptoms intravenous <a href="/wiki/Atropine" title="Atropine">atropine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emetics" class="mw-redirect" title="Emetics">emetics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Stomach_pumping" class="mw-redirect" title="Stomach pumping">stomach pumping</a> may be indicated. However, ingestion of large quantities accidentally is unusual because of a strong unpleasant taste. When narcissi were compared with a number of other plants not normally consumed by animals, narcissi were the most repellent, specifically  <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> Consequently, narcissus alkaloids have been used as repellents and may also discourage fungi, molds and bacteria.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 1 May 2009, a number of schoolchildren fell ill at Gorseland Primary School in <a href="/wiki/Martlesham_Heath" title="Martlesham Heath">Martlesham Heath</a>, Suffolk, England, after a daffodil bulb was added to soup during a cookery class.<sup id="cite_ref-Pupils_ill_after_bulb_put_in_soup_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pupils_ill_after_bulb_put_in_soup-184">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topical_effects">Topical effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Topical effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>One of the most common <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">dermatitis</a> problems for flower pickers, packers, florists and gardeners, "daffodil itch", involves dryness, fissures, scaling, and <a href="/wiki/Erythema" title="Erythema">erythema</a> in the hands, often accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Ungual" title="Ungual">subungual</a> <a href="/wiki/Hyperkeratosis" title="Hyperkeratosis">hyperkeratosis</a> (thickening of the skin beneath the nails). It is blamed on exposure to <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxalate" title="Calcium oxalate">calcium oxalate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelidonic_acid" title="Chelidonic acid">chelidonic acid</a> or alkaloids such as lycorine in the sap, either due to a direct irritant effect or an allergic reaction.<sup id="cite_ref-Floridata_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Floridata-187">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Botanical_Dermatology_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Botanical_Dermatology-188">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> It has long been recognised that some cultivars provoke dermatitis more readily than others. <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i> and the <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> "Actaea", "Camparelle", "Gloriosa", "Grande Monarque", "Ornatus", "Princeps" and "Scilly White" are known to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-189">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>If bulb extracts come into contact with wounds, both central nervous system and cardiac symptoms may result. The scent can also cause toxic reactions such as headaches and vomiting from <i>N. bulbocodium</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_medicine">Traditional medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Traditional medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Despite the lethal potential of <i>Narcissus</i> alkaloids, they have been used for centuries as <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicines</a> for a variety of complaints, including cancer. Plants thought to be <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_poeticus" title="Narcissus poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i>  and <i><a href="/wiki/N._tazetta" class="mw-redirect" title="N. tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i> are described in the Bible in the treatment for what is thought to be cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-Kornienko_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kornienko-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAtta-ur-Rahman1998&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidWjy24u4ubiUCpgPA391_Bastida_&#39;&#39;et_al.&#39;&#39;_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_alkaloids_p.&amp;nbsp;391&#93;_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAtta-ur-Rahman1998[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidWjy24u4ubiUCpgPA391_Bastida_&#39;&#39;et_al.&#39;&#39;_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;_alkaloids_p.&amp;nbsp;391]-190">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettit_et_al.1990_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettit_et_al.1990-191">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwell1967_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwell1967-192">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Greek</a> world <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> (ca. B.C. 460\xe2\x80\x93370) recommended a pessary prepared from narcissus oil for <a href="/wiki/Uterine_tumors" class="mw-redirect" title="Uterine tumors">uterine tumors</a>, a practice continued by <a href="/wiki/Pedanius_Dioscorides" title="Pedanius Dioscorides">Pedanius Dioscorides</a> (ca. A.D. 40\xe2\x80\x9390) and <a href="/wiki/Soranus_of_Ephesus" title="Soranus of Ephesus">Soranus of Ephesus</a> (A.D. 98\xe2\x80\x93138) in the first and second centuries A.D., while the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> (A.D. 23\xe2\x80\x9379), advocated <a href="/wiki/Topical" class="mw-redirect" title="Topical">topical</a> use.<sup id="cite_ref-Kornienko_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kornienko-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The bulbs of <i>N. poeticus</i> contain the <a href="/wiki/Antineoplastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Antineoplastic">antineoplastic</a> agent narciclasine. This usage is also found in later <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabian</a>, North African, Central American and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people" title="Chinese people">Chinese</a> medicine during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kornienko_186-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kornienko-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> In China <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_tazetta" title="Narcissus tazetta">N. tazetta</a> var. chinensis was grown as an ornamental plant but the bulbs were applied topically to tumors in traditional folk medicine. These bulbs contain pretazettine, an active antitumor compound.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwell1967_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwell1967-192">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ingrassia_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ingrassia-193">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Narcissus</i> products have received a variety of other uses. The Roman physician <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Cornelius_Celsus" title="Aulus Cornelius Celsus">Aulus Cornelius Celsus</a> listed narcissus root in <i><a href="/wiki/De_Medicina" title="De Medicina">De Medicina</a></i> among <a href="/wiki/Herbalism" title="Herbalism">medical herbs</a>, described as <a href="/wiki/Moisturizer" title="Moisturizer">emollient</a>, erodent, and "powerful to disperse whatever has collected in any part of the body". N. tazetta bulbs were used in Turkey as a remedy for abscesses in the belief they were <a href="/wiki/Antiphlogistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiphlogistic">antiphlogistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">analgesic</a>. Other uses include the application to wounds, strains, painful joints and various local ailments as an <a href="/wiki/Ointment" class="mw-redirect" title="Ointment">ointment</a> called \'Narcissimum\'. Powdered flowers have also been used medically, as an <a href="/wiki/Emetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Emetic">emetic</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Decongestant" title="Decongestant">decongestant</a> and for the relief of <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a>, in the form of a syrup or <a href="/wiki/Infusion" title="Infusion">infusion</a>. The French used the flowers as an <a href="/wiki/Antispasmodic" title="Antispasmodic">antispasmodic</a>, the Arabs the oil for <a href="/wiki/Baldness" class="mw-redirect" title="Baldness">baldness</a> and also an <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">aphrodisiac</a>. In the eighteenth century the Irish <a href="/wiki/Herbal" title="Herbal">herbal</a> of <a href="/wiki/John_K%27Eogh" title="John K&#39;Eogh">John K\'Eogh</a> recommended pounding the roots in honey for use on burns, bruises, <a href="/wiki/Joint_dislocation" title="Joint dislocation">dislocations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freckles" class="mw-redirect" title="Freckles">freckles</a>, and for drawing out thorns and splinters. <i>N. tazetta</i> bulbs have also been used for <a href="/wiki/Contraception" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraception">contraception</a>, while the flowers have been recommended for <a href="/wiki/Hysteria" title="Hysteria">hysteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Homeopathic" class="mw-redirect" title="Homeopathic">homeopathic</a> medicine made from bulbs was prescribed for <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whooping_cough" title="Whooping cough">whooping cough</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In the traditional Japanese medicine of <a href="/wiki/Kampo" title="Kampo">kampo</a>, wounds were treated with narcissus root and <a href="/wiki/Wheatpaste" title="Wheatpaste">wheat flour paste</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Altomonte_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altomonte-194">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> the plant, however, does not appear in the modern <a href="/wiki/Kampo_herb_list" class="mw-redirect" title="Kampo herb list">kampo herb list</a>.\n</p><p>There is also a long history of the use of <i>Narcissus</i> as a <a href="/wiki/Stimulant" title="Stimulant">stimulant</a> and to induce <a href="/wiki/Trance" title="Trance">trance</a> like states and <a href="/wiki/Hallucinations" class="mw-redirect" title="Hallucinations">hallucinations</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a> referred to the narcissus as the "Chaplet of the infernal Gods",<sup id="cite_ref-Prior_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prior-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> a statement frequently wrongly attributed to <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> (see <a href="#Antiquity">Antiquity</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_properties">Biological properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Biological properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Extracts of <i>Narcissus</i> have demonstrated a number of potentially useful biological properties including <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">antiviral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prophage_induction" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophage induction">prophage induction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antibacterial" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibacterial">antibacterial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">antifungal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimalarial" class="mw-redirect" title="Antimalarial">antimalarial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insecticidal" title="Insecticidal">insecticidal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytotoxic">cytotoxic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antitumor" class="mw-redirect" title="Antitumor">antitumor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimitotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Antimitotic">antimitotic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antiplatelet" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiplatelet">antiplatelet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypotensive" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypotensive">hypotensive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Emetic">emetic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine_esterase_inhibitory" class="mw-redirect" title="Acetylcholine esterase inhibitory">acetylcholine esterase inhibitory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antifertility" class="mw-redirect" title="Antifertility">antifertility</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antinociceptive" class="mw-redirect" title="Antinociceptive">antinociceptive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chronotropic" title="Chronotropic">chronotropic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant_growth_inhibitor" class="mw-redirect" title="Plant growth inhibitor">plant growth inhibitor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Allelopathic" class="mw-redirect" title="Allelopathic">allelopathic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> extract of <i>Narcissus</i> bulbs was found effective in one mouse model of <a href="/wiki/Nociception" title="Nociception">nociception</a>, para-<a href="/wiki/Benzoquinone" title="Benzoquinone">benzoquinone</a> induced abdominal constriction, but not in another, the hot plate test.<sup id="cite_ref-Cakici_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cakici-195">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Most of these properties are due to alkaloids, but some are also due to <a href="/wiki/Mannosa-binding_lectins" class="mw-redirect" title="Mannosa-binding lectins">mannosa-binding lectins</a>. The most-studied alkaloids in this group are <a href="/wiki/Galantamine" title="Galantamine">galantamine</a> (galanthamine),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECID96512015_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECID96512015-196">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lycorine" title="Lycorine">lycorine</a>, narciclasine, and pretazettine.\n</p><p>It is likely that the traditional use of narcissi for the treatment of cancer was due to the presence of isocarbostyril constituents such as <a href="/wiki/Narciclasine" title="Narciclasine">narciclasine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancratistatin" title="Pancratistatin">pancratistatin</a> and their congeners. <i>N. poeticus</i> contains about 0.12g of narciclasine per kg of fresh bulbs.<sup id="cite_ref-Kornienko_186-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kornienko-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Acetylcholine esterase inhibition has attracted the most interest as a possible therapeutic intervention, with activity varying by a thousandfold between species, and the greatest activity seen in those that contain galantamine or epinorgalanthamine.<sup id="cite_ref-Ronsted2008_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ronsted2008-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodent</a> repellant properties of <i>Narcissus</i> alkaloids have been utilised in horticulture to protect more vulnerable bulbs.<sup id="cite_ref-Protectrodent_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Protectrodent-197">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Therapeutics">Therapeutics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Therapeutics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Field_of_Daffodils_-_Pentre_Meyrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1260139.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Field_of_Daffodils_-_Pentre_Meyrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1260139.jpg/220px-Field_of_Daffodils_-_Pentre_Meyrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1260139.jpg" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Field_of_Daffodils_-_Pentre_Meyrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1260139.jpg/330px-Field_of_Daffodils_-_Pentre_Meyrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1260139.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Field_of_Daffodils_-_Pentre_Meyrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1260139.jpg/440px-Field_of_Daffodils_-_Pentre_Meyrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1260139.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Field_of_Daffodils_-_Pentre_Meyrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1260139.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Daffodils growing in Wales</div></div></div>\n<p>Of all the alkaloids, only galantamine has made it to therapeutic use in man, as the drug galantamine for <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Alzheimer&#39;s">Alzheimer\'s</a> disease. Galantamine is an acetylcholine esterase inhibitor which crosses the <a href="/wiki/Blood_brain_barrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood brain barrier">blood brain barrier</a> and is active within the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Daffodils are grown commercially near <a href="/wiki/Brecon" title="Brecon">Brecon</a> in Powys, Wales, to produce <a href="/wiki/Galantamine" title="Galantamine">galantamine</a>, a drug used to combat <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer&#39;s disease">Alzheimer\'s disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2008_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2008-198">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_uses">Commercial uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Commercial uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Throughout history the scent of narcissi has been an important ingredient of <a href="/wiki/Perfumes" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfumes">perfumes</a>, a quality that comes from <a href="/wiki/Essential_oils" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential oils">essential oils</a> rather than alkaloids.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Narcissi are also an important <a href="/wiki/Horticultural" class="mw-redirect" title="Horticultural">horticultural</a> crop,<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld_53-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dweck_73-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dweck-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and source of <a href="/wiki/Cut_flowers" title="Cut flowers">cut flowers</a> (<a href="/wiki/Floriculture" title="Floriculture">floriculture</a>).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_field_near_Keukenhof.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Narcissus_field_near_Keukenhof.jpg/220px-Narcissus_field_near_Keukenhof.jpg" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Narcissus_field_near_Keukenhof.jpg/330px-Narcissus_field_near_Keukenhof.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Narcissus_field_near_Keukenhof.jpg/440px-Narcissus_field_near_Keukenhof.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2316" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_field_near_Keukenhof.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Daffodil production in the Netherlands</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, which is the most important source of flower bulbs worldwide is also a major centre of narcissus production. Of 16,700 hectares (ha) under cultivation for flower bulbs, narcissi account for about 1,800 hectares. In the 1990s narcissus bulb production was at 260 million, sixth in size after <a href="/wiki/Tulips" class="mw-redirect" title="Tulips">tulips</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gladioli" class="mw-redirect" title="Gladioli">gladioli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iris_(plant)" title="Iris (plant)">irises</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crocuses" class="mw-redirect" title="Crocuses">crocuses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lilies" class="mw-redirect" title="Lilies">lilies</a> and in 2012 it was ranked third.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA103_Narcissi&#93;,_p._103_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA103_Narcissi],_p._103-144">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> About two-thirds of the area under cultivation is dedicated to about 20 of the most popular varieties. In the 2009/2010 season 470 cultivars were produced on 1578&#160;ha. By far the largest area cultivated is for the miniature "T\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-T\xc3\xaate", followed at some distance by "Carlton". The largest production cultivars are shown in Table II;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA106_Narcissi&#93;,_p._106_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA106_Narcissi],_p._106-199">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<caption><big> Table II: Area under cultivation in the Netherlands, by cultivar</big>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Cultivar</th>\n<th>Division</th>\n<th>Colour</th>\n<th>Area (ha)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>"T\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-T\xc3\xaate"</td>\n<td>6: Cyclamineus</td>\n<td>Yellow</td>\n<td>663\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>"Carlton"</td>\n<td>2: Large cup</td>\n<td>Yellow</td>\n<td>54\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>"Bridal Crown"</td>\n<td>4: Double</td>\n<td>White\xe2\x80\x93Yellow</td>\n<td>51\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>"Dutch Master"</td>\n<td>1: Trumpet</td>\n<td>Yellow</td>\n<td>47\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>"Jetfire"</td>\n<td>6: Cyclamineus</td>\n<td>Yellow\xe2\x80\x93Orange</td>\n<td>42\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>"Ice Follies"</td>\n<td>2: Large cup</td>\n<td>White</td>\n<td>36\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>"Carlton" and "Ice Follies" (Division 2: Large cup) have a long history of cultivation, together with "Dutch Master" and "Golden Harvest" (1: yellow). "Carlton" and "Golden Harvest" were introduced in 1927, and "Ice Follies" in 1953. "Carlton", with over 9000 million bulbs (350 000 tons), is among the more numerous individual plants produced in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMabberley2008&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZFFgAgAAQBAJpgPA571_Narcissus&#93;,_p._571_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMabberley2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZFFgAgAAQBAJpgPA571_Narcissus],_p._571-200">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> The other major areas of production are the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA103_Narcissi&#93;,_p._103_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA103_Narcissi],_p._103-144">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> which exported 25 million <i><a href="/wiki/N._tazetta" class="mw-redirect" title="N. tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i> cultivar bulbs in 2003,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA106_Narcissi&#93;,_p._106_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA106_Narcissi],_p._106-199">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.\n</p><p>In the United Kingdom a total of 4100&#160;ha were planted with bulbs, of which 3800&#160;ha were Narcissi, the UK\'s most important bulb crop, much of which is for export,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlford2000&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrxw5JvD7MN4CpgPA542_p._542&#93;_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlford2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrxw5JvD7MN4CpgPA542_p._542]-201">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> making this the largest global production centre, about half of the total production area. While some of the production is for forcing, most is for dry bulb production. Bulb production and forcing occurs in the East, while production in the south west is mainly for outdoor flower production.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlford2000&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrxw5JvD7MN4CpgPA551_p._551&#93;_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlford2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrxw5JvD7MN4CpgPA551_p._551]-202">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Farm_gate_value" title="Farm gate value">farm gate value</a> was estimated at \xc2\xa310m in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFellows2007_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFellows2007-203">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kuekenhoff_006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Kuekenhoff_006.jpg/220px-Kuekenhoff_006.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Kuekenhoff_006.jpg/330px-Kuekenhoff_006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Kuekenhoff_006.jpg/440px-Kuekenhoff_006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kuekenhoff_006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Narcissi growing at <a href="/wiki/Keukenhof" title="Keukenhof">Keukenhof</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Production of both bulbs and cut flowers takes place in open fields in beds or ridges, often in the same field, allowing adaptation to changing market conditions. Narcissi grow best in mild maritime climates. Compared to the United Kingdom, the harsher winters in the Netherlands require covering the fields with straw for protection. Areas with higher rainfall and temperatures are more susceptible to <a href="#Pests_and_diseases">diseases</a> that attack the crops. production is based on a 1 (UK) or 2 (Netherlands) year cycle. Optimal soil <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> is 6.0\xe2\x80\x937.5. Prior to planting disinfection by hot water takes place, such as immersion at 44.4&#160;\xc2\xb0C for three hours.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi&#93;,_p._107_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi],_p._107-135">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bulbs are harvested for market in the summer, sorted, stored for 2\xe2\x80\x933 weeks, and then further disinfected by a hot (43.5&#160;\xc2\xb0C) bath. This eliminates infestations by narcissus fly and nematodes. The bulbs are then dried at a high temperature, and then stored at 15.5&#160;\xc2\xb0C.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The initiation of new flower development in the bulb takes place in late spring before the bulbs are lifted, and is completed by mid summer while the bulbs are in storage. The optimal temperature for initiation is 20&#160;\xc2\xb0C followed by cooling to 13&#160;\xc2\xb0C.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi&#93;,_p._104_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA104_Narcissi],_p._104-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Traditionally, sales took place in the daffodil fields prior to harvesting the bulbs, but today sales are handled by Marketing Boards although still before harvesting. In the Netherlands there are special exhibition gardens for major buyers to view flowers and order bulbs, some larger ones may have more than a thousand narcissus varieties on display. While individuals can visit these gardens they cannot buy bulbs at <a href="/wiki/Retail" title="Retail">retail</a>, which are only available at <a href="/wiki/Wholesale" class="mw-redirect" title="Wholesale">wholesale</a>, usually at a minimum of several <a href="/wiki/Hundredweight" title="Hundredweight">hundredweight</a>. The most famous display is at <a href="/wiki/Keukenhof" title="Keukenhof">Keukenhof</a>, although only about 100 narcissus varieties are on display there.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Forcing">Forcing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Forcing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There is also a market for forced blooms, both as cut flowers and potted flowers through the winter from Christmas to Easter, the long season requiring special preparation by growers.\n</p>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Cut_flowers" title="Cut flowers">Cut flowers</a></dt>\n<dd>For <a href="/wiki/Cut_flowers" title="Cut flowers">cut flowers</a>, bulbs larger than 12&#160;cm in size are preferred. To bloom in December, bulbs are harvested in June to July, dried, stored for four days at 34&#160;\xc2\xb0C, two weeks at 30 and two weeks at 17\xe2\x80\x9320&#160;\xc2\xb0C and then placed in cold storage for precooling at 9 degrees for about 15\xe2\x80\x9316 weeks. The bulbs are then planted in light compost in crates in a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouse</a> for forcing at 13&#160;\xc2\xb0C\xe2\x80\x9315&#160;\xc2\xb0C and the blooms appear in 19\xe2\x80\x9330 days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanks2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;&#93;,_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329_6-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanks2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4_kH_BffGDgCpgPA1_Hanks,_&quot;The_biology_of_&#39;&#39;Narcissus&#39;&#39;&quot;],_pp._1\xe2\x80\x9329-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi&#93;,_p._107_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi],_p._107-135">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt>Potted flowers</dt>\n<dd>For potted flowers a lower temperature is used for precooling (5&#160;\xc2\xb0C for 15 weeks), followed by 16&#160;\xc2\xb0C\xe2\x80\x9318&#160;\xc2\xb0C in a greenhouse. For later blooming (mid- and late-forcing), bulbs are harvested in July to August and the higher temperatures are omitted, being stored a 17\xe2\x80\x9320&#160;\xc2\xb0C after harvesting and placed in cold storage at 9&#160;\xc2\xb0C in September for 17\xe2\x80\x9318 (cut flowers) or 14\xe2\x80\x9316 (potted flowers) weeks. The bulbs can then be planted in cold frames, and then forced in a greenhouse according to requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi&#93;,_p._107_135-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA107_Narcissi],_p._107-135">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt><i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_tazetta" title="Narcissus tazetta">Narcissus tazetta</a></i></dt>\n<dd><i><a href="/wiki/N._tazetta" class="mw-redirect" title="N. tazetta">N. tazetta</a></i> and its cultivars are an exception to this rule, requiring no cold period. Often harvested in October, bulbs are lifted in May and dried and heated to 30&#160;\xc2\xb0C for three weeks, then stored at 25&#160;\xc2\xb0C for 12 weeks and planted. Flowering can be delayed by storing at 5&#160;\xc2\xb0C\xe2\x80\x9310&#160;\xc2\xb0C.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi&#93;,_p._108_106-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamenetsky2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5B-ucdbgA3wCpgPA108_Narcissi],_p._108-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_in_culture" title="Narcissus in culture">Narcissus in culture</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_%27Thalia%27.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Narcissus_%27Thalia%27.jpg/220px-Narcissus_%27Thalia%27.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Narcissus_%27Thalia%27.jpg/330px-Narcissus_%27Thalia%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Narcissus_%27Thalia%27.jpg/440px-Narcissus_%27Thalia%27.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_%27Thalia%27.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_triandrus" title="Narcissus triandrus">N. triandrus</a> \'Thalia\', considered a grave flower</div></div></div>\n<p>The daffodil is the <a href="/wiki/National_symbols_of_Wales" title="National symbols of Wales">national flower</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, associated with <a href="/wiki/Saint_David%27s_Day" title="Saint David&#39;s Day">Saint David\'s Day</a> (March 1). The narcissus is also a national flower symbolising the new year or <i>Newroz</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian culture">Iranian culture</a>.\n</p><p>In the West the narcissus is perceived  as a symbol of vanity, in the East as a symbol of wealth and good fortune (see <a href="#Eastern_cultures">Eastern cultures</a>), while in <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian literature</a>, the narcissus is a symbol of beautiful eyes.\n</p><p>In western countries the daffodil is also associated with spring festivals such as <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a> and its successor <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>. In Germany the wild narcissus, <i>N. pseudonarcissus</i>, is known as the <i><a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osterglocke" class="extiw" title="de:Osterglocke">Osterglocke</a></i> or "Easter bell." In the United Kingdom the daffodil is sometimes referred to as the Lenten  lily.<sup id="cite_ref-Hereford_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hereford-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oxford_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;Note 4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although prized as an ornamental flower, some people consider narcissi unlucky, because they hang their heads implying misfortune.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastida_24-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastida-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> White narcissi, such as <i>N triandrus</i> "Thalia", are especially associated with death, and have been called grave flowers.<sup id="cite_ref-Paghat_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paghat-205">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kandeler_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kandeler-206">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> In Ancient Greece narcissi were planted near tombs, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Herrick_(poet)" title="Robert Herrick (poet)">Robert Herrick</a> describes them as portents of death, an association which also appears in the myth of <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Persephone</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Underworld" title="Underworld">underworld</a> (see <a href="#The_Arts">The Arts, below</a>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Demeter_rejoiced,_for_her_daughter_was_by_her_side.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Demeter_rejoiced%2C_for_her_daughter_was_by_her_side.jpg/220px-Demeter_rejoiced%2C_for_her_daughter_was_by_her_side.jpg" width="220" height="327" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Demeter_rejoiced%2C_for_her_daughter_was_by_her_side.jpg/330px-Demeter_rejoiced%2C_for_her_daughter_was_by_her_side.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Demeter_rejoiced%2C_for_her_daughter_was_by_her_side.jpg/440px-Demeter_rejoiced%2C_for_her_daughter_was_by_her_side.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2191" data-file-height="3257" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Demeter_rejoiced,_for_her_daughter_was_by_her_side.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Persephone</a> surrounded by daffodils - "<i>Demeter rejoiced, for her daughter was by her side</i>"</div></div></div>\n<p>The decorative use of narcissi dates as far back as <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Egyptian">ancient Egyptian</a> tombs,  and <a href="/wiki/Frescoes" class="mw-redirect" title="Frescoes">frescoes</a> at <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJashemskiMeyer2002131_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJashemskiMeyer2002131-207">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> They are mentioned in the King James Version of the Bible<sup id="cite_ref-Isaiah_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Isaiah-208">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> as the <a href="/wiki/Rose_of_Sharon" title="Rose of Sharon">Rose of Sharon</a><sup id="cite_ref-Dweck_73-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dweck-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McClintock_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McClintock-209">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WFI1_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WFI1-210">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WFI2_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WFI2-211">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> and make frequent appearances in classical literature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHale1757&#91;httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem131789page585mode1up_pp._495-496&#93;_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHale1757[httpswwwbiodiversitylibraryorgitem131789page585mode1up_pp._495-496]-161">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_culture">Greek culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Greek culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The narcissus appears in two <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Graeco-Roman">Graeco-Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Myths" class="mw-redirect" title="Myths">myths</a>, that of the youth <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(mythology)" title="Narcissus (mythology)">Narcissus</a> who was turned into the flower of that name, and of the Goddess <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Persephone</a> <a href="/wiki/Rape_of_Persephone" title="Rape of Persephone">snatched</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Greek_underworld" title="Greek underworld">Underworld</a> by the god <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a> while picking the flowers.
