The narcissus is considered sacred to both Hades and Persephone,<sup id="cite_ref-Ruskin_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruskin-212">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> and to grow along the banks of the river <a href="/wiki/Styx" title="Styx">Styx</a> in the underworld.<sup id="cite_ref-Kandeler_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kandeler-206">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Greek poet <a href="/wiki/Stasinos" class="mw-redirect" title="Stasinos">Stasinos</a> mentioned them in the <i><a href="/wiki/Cypria" title="Cypria">Cypria</a></i> amongst the flowers of Cyprus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECyrino2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddhv2AV3eTsUCpgPA63_p._63&#93;_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECyrino2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddhv2AV3eTsUCpgPA63_p._63]-213">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> The legend of Persephone comes to us mainly in the seventh century BC <a href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymn" class="mw-redirect" title="Homeric Hymn">Homeric Hymn</a> <i>To Demeter</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-DemeterNagy_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DemeterNagy-214">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> where the author describes the narcissus, and its role as a lure to trap the young Persephone. The flower, she recounts to her mother was the last flower she reached for before being seized.\n</p><p>Other Greek authors making reference to the narcissus include <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>. Sophocles, in <i><a href="/wiki/Oedipus_at_Colonus" title="Oedipus at Colonus">Oedipus at Colonus</a></i> utilises narcissus in a symbolic manner, implying fertility,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkantonatos2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfaciAAAAQBAJpgPA186_p._186&#93;_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkantonatos2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfaciAAAAQBAJpgPA186_p._186]-215">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> allying it with the cults of <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a> and her daughter <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Kore (Persephone)</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-OedCol_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OedCol-216">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> and by extension, a symbol of death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkantonatos2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfaciAAAAQBAJpgPA206_pp._206_\xe2\x80\x93_207&#93;_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkantonatos2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfaciAAAAQBAJpgPA206_pp._206_\xe2\x80\x93_207]-217">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Claverhouse_Jebb" title="Richard Claverhouse Jebb">Jebb</a> comments that it is <i>the flower of imminent death</i> with its fragrance being narcotic, emphasised by its pale white colour. Just as Persephone reaching for the flower heralded her doom, the youth Narcissus gazing at his own reflection portended his own death.<sup id="cite_ref-OedCol_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OedCol-216">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Plutarch refers to this in his <i>Symposiacs</i> as numbing the nerves causing a heaviness in the limbs.<sup id="cite_ref-Plutarch_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plutarch-218">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> He refers to Sophocles\' "crown of the great Goddesses", which is the source of the English phrase "<a href="/wiki/Corolla_(chaplet)" title="Corolla (chaplet)">Chaplet</a> of the infernal Gods" incorrectly attributed to <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Prior_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prior-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A passage by <a href="/wiki/Moschus" title="Moschus">Moschus</a>, describes fragrant narcissi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoschus1919l._65_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoschus1919l._65-219">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarley1877_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarley1877-220">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheocritusBionMoschus1880&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid3okOAAAAQAAJpgPA183_Moschus._Idyll_II,_Europa_and_the_Bull_(pp._181\xe2\x80\x93187)_at_p._183&#93;_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheocritusBionMoschus1880[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid3okOAAAAQAAJpgPA183_Moschus._Idyll_II,_Europa_and_the_Bull_(pp._181\xe2\x80\x93187)_at_p._183]-221">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>  in his <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Homer1_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Homer1-222">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HomerGrk1_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HomerGrk1-223">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Homer2_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Homer2-224">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HomerGrk2_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HomerGrk2-225">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> described the underworld as having <a href="/wiki/Elysian" class="mw-redirect" title="Elysian">Elysian</a> meadows carpeted with flowers, thought to be narcissus, as described by <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dweck_73-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dweck-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster2014&#91;httpwwwmerriam-webstercomdictionaryasphodel_Asphodel&#93;_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster2014[httpwwwmerriam-webstercomdictionaryasphodel_Asphodel]-226">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;Note 5&#93;</a></sup> A similar account is provided by <a href="/wiki/Lucian" title="Lucian">Lucian</a> describing the flowers in the underworld.<sup id="cite_ref-Lucian1_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lucian1-228">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lucian2_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lucian2-229">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LucianEng_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LucianEng-230">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> The myth of the youth Narcissus is also taken up by <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a>. He believed that the myth of Persephone long antedated that of Narcissus, and hence discounts the idea the flower was named after the youth.<sup id="cite_ref-Pausanias_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pausanias-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_culture">Roman culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Roman culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, the first known <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> writer to refer to the narcissus, does so in several places, for instance twice in the <i><a href="/wiki/Georgics" title="Georgics">Georgics</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-DavidsonVirgil1_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidsonVirgil1-231">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> Virgil refers to the cup shaped corona of the narcissus flower, allegedly containing the tears of the self-loving youth <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(mythology)" title="Narcissus (mythology)">Narcissus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DavidsonVirgil2_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidsonVirgil2-232">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">Milton</a> makes a similar analogy "<i>And Daffodillies fill their Cups with Tears</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-Lycidas_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycidas-233">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Virgil also mentions narcissi three times in the <i><a href="/wiki/Eclogues" title="Eclogues">Eclogues</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-DavidsonVirgil5_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidsonVirgil5-234">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DavidsonVirgil3_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidsonVirgil3-235">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The poet <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> also dealt with the mythology of the narcissus. In his <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i>, he recounts the story of the youth Narcissus who, after his death, is turned into the flower,<sup id="cite_ref-Ovidmet_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ovidmet-236">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Klinemet_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klinemet-237">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> and it is also mentioned in Book 5 of his poem <i><a href="/wiki/Fasti_(poem)" title="Fasti (poem)">Fasti</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ovidfast_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ovidfast-238">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Klinefast_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klinefast-239">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> This theme of metamorphosis was broader than just <i>Narcissus</i>; for instance see <a href="/wiki/Crocus" title="Crocus">crocus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laurus_nobilis" title="Laurus nobilis">laurel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hyacinth_(plant)" title="Hyacinth (plant)">hyacinth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Panofsky_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Panofsky-240">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_culture">Western culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Western culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:20%; border-width: 2px; font-size: 75%; background-color: Cornsilk;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-title" style="background-color: Cornsilk; color: blue;"><i>I wandered lonely as a Cloud</i></div>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style=""><div class="poem">\n<p><br /><a href="/wiki/File:Decorative_I.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Decorative I.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Decorative_I.jpg/15px-Decorative_I.jpg" width="15" height="15" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Decorative_I.jpg/23px-Decorative_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Decorative_I.jpg/30px-Decorative_I.jpg 2x" data-file-width="107" data-file-height="105" /></a> wandered lonely as a Cloud<br />\nThat floats on high o\'er Vales and Hills,<br />\nWhen all at once I saw a crowd<br />\nA host of dancing Daffodils;<br />\nAlong the Lake, beneath the trees,<br />\nTen thousand dancing in the breeze.<br />\n<br />\nThe waves beside them danced, but they<br />\nOutdid the sparkling waves in glee: \xe2\x80\x93<br />\nA poet could not but be gay<br />\nIn such a laughing company:<br />\nI gaz\'d \xe2\x80\x93 and gaz\'d \xe2\x80\x93 but little thought<br />\nWhat wealth the shew to me had brought:<br />\n<br />\nFor oft when on my couch I lie<br />\nIn vacant or in pensive mood,<br />\nThey flash upon that inward eye<br />\nWhich is the bliss of solitude,<br />\nAnd then my heart with pleasure fills,<br />\nAnd dances with the Daffodils.\n</p>\n</div></div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><i>William Wordsworth (1804 version)</i><sup id="cite_ref-WW1_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WW1-241">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>\n</cite></p>\n<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:282_Hager_Bot_Unterricht_1885.png" class="image"><img alt="282 Hager Bot Unterricht 1885.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/282_Hager_Bot_Unterricht_1885.png/30px-282_Hager_Bot_Unterricht_1885.png" width="30" height="23" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/282_Hager_Bot_Unterricht_1885.png/45px-282_Hager_Bot_Unterricht_1885.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/282_Hager_Bot_Unterricht_1885.png/60px-282_Hager_Bot_Unterricht_1885.png 2x" data-file-width="399" data-file-height="302" /></a></div></div>\n</div>\n<p>Although there is no clear evidence that the flower\'s name derives directly from the Greek myth, this link between the flower and the myth became firmly part of western culture. The narcissus or daffodil is the most loved of all English plants,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA73_Daffodils&#93;,_p._73_150-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA73_Daffodils],_p._73-150">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> and appears frequently in English literature. Many English writers have referred to the cultural and symbolic importance of <i>Narcissus</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnonymous1823_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnonymous1823-242">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurbidge1875_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurbidge1875-243">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrBurbidge1884-244">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884-245">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>). No flower has received more poetic description except the <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">rose</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lily" class="mw-redirect" title="Lily">lily</a>, with poems by authors from <a href="/wiki/John_Gower" title="John Gower">John Gower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>, Milton (see <a href="#Roman_culture">Roman culture</a>, above), <a href="/wiki/Wordsworth" class="mw-redirect" title="Wordsworth">Wordsworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Shelley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keats" class="mw-redirect" title="Keats">Keats</a>. Frequently the poems deal with self-love derived from Ovid\'s account.<sup id="cite_ref-PoetryNarcissus_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PoetryNarcissus-246">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA74_Daffodils&#93;,_p._4&#93;_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA74_Daffodils],_p._4]-247">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Gower\'s reference to the yellow flower of the legend has been assumed to be the daffodil or <i>Narcissus</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA74_Daffofils&#93;,_p._74_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA74_Daffofils],_p._74-248">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> though as with all references in the older literature to the flower that sprang from the youth\'s death, there is room for some debate as to the exact species of flower indicated, some preferring <i><a href="/wiki/Crocus" title="Crocus">Crocus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYeager1990&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidSmH4Q4GMyHECpgPA133_Transformations&#93;,_pp._133\xe2\x80\x93135_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYeager1990[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidSmH4Q4GMyHECpgPA133_Transformations],_pp._133\xe2\x80\x93135-249">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Spenser announces the coming of the Daffodil in <i>Aprill</i> of his <i><a href="/wiki/Shepheardes_Calender" class="mw-redirect" title="Shepheardes Calender">Shepheardes Calender</a></i> (1579).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpenser1579&#91;httpwwwluminariumorgrenascence-editionsaprillhtml_Aprill_&#39;&#39;l.&#39;&#39;_140&#93;_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpenser1579[httpwwwluminariumorgrenascence-editionsaprillhtml_Aprill_&#39;&#39;l.&#39;&#39;_140]-250">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Shakespeare, <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare_garden" title="Shakespeare garden">who frequently uses flower imagery</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884-245">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> refers to daffodils twice in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Winter%27s_Tale" title="The Winter&#39;s Tale">The Winter\'s Tale</a></i> <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShakespeare1623Perdita,_IV_4_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShakespeare1623Perdita,_IV_4-251">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> and also <i><a href="/wiki/The_Two_Noble_Kinsmen" title="The Two Noble Kinsmen">The Two Noble Kinsmen</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Herrick_(poet)" title="Robert Herrick (poet)">Robert Herrick</a> alludes to their association with death in a number of poems.<sup id="cite_ref-Herrick2_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herrick2-252">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Herrick1_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herrick1-253">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Among the English <a href="/wiki/Romantic_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Romantic movement">romantic movement</a> writers none is better known than <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>\'s short 1804 poem <i><a href="/wiki/I_Wandered_Lonely_as_a_Cloud" title="I Wandered Lonely as a Cloud">I Wandered Lonely as a Cloud</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-WW1_241-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WW1-241">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> which has become linked in the popular mind with the daffodils that form its main image.<sup id="cite_ref-Dweck_73-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dweck-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kandeler_206-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kandeler-206">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PoetryNarcissus_246-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PoetryNarcissus-246">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WTrust_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WTrust-254">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> Wordsworth also included the daffodil in other poems.<sup id="cite_ref-WW2_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WW2-255">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Yet the description given of daffodils by his sister, <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Wordsworth" title="Dorothy Wordsworth">Dorothy</a> is just as poetic, if not more so,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA76_Daffodils&#93;,_p._76_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA76_Daffodils],_p._76-167">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> just that her poetry was prose and appears almost an unconscious imitation of the first section of the Homeric <i>Hymn to Demeter</i> (see <a href="#Greek_culture">Greek culture</a>, above).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELQR1853&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidSJTfAAAAMAAJpgPA112_&#39;&#39;Memoirs_of_Wordsworth&#39;&#39;&#93;,_Issue_183,_January,_p._112_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELQR1853[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidSJTfAAAAMAAJpgPA112_&#39;&#39;Memoirs_of_Wordsworth&#39;&#39;],_Issue_183,_January,_p._112-256">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA76_Daffodils&#93;,_p._76_167-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllacombe1884[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidautIAAAAIAAJpgPA76_Daffodils],_p._76-167">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWordsworth1802_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWordsworth1802-257">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> Among their contemporaries, Keats refers to daffodils among those things capable of bringing "joy for ever".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeats1818_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeats1818-258">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>More recently <a href="/wiki/A._E._Housman" title="A. E. Housman">A. E. Housman</a>, using one of the daffodil\'s more symbolic names (see <a href="#Symbols">Symbols</a>), wrote <i>The Lent Lily</i> in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Shropshire_Lad" title="A Shropshire Lad">A Shropshire Lad</a></i>, describing the traditional Easter death of the daffodil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHousman1896XXIX_&#39;&#39;The_Lent_Lily&#39;&#39;,_1896_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHousman1896XXIX_&#39;&#39;The_Lent_Lily&#39;&#39;,_1896-259">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In  <i>Black Narcissus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Rumer_Godden" title="Rumer Godden">Rumer Godden</a> describes the disorientation of English nuns in the Indian <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>, and gives the plant name an unexpected twist, alluding both to <a href="/wiki/Narcissism" title="Narcissism">narcissism</a> and the effect of the perfume <i>Narcisse Noir</i> (<a href="/wiki/Parfums_Caron" title="Parfums Caron">Caron</a>) on others. The novel was later adapted into the <a href="/wiki/Black_Narcissus" title="Black Narcissus">1947 British film of the same name</a>. The narcissus also appears in German literature such as that of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gerhardt" title="Paul Gerhardt">Paul Gerhardt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gerhardt_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gerhardt-260">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the visual arts, narcissi are depicted in three different contexts, mythological (Narcissus, Persephone), floral art, or landscapes. The Narcissus story has been popular with painters and the youth is frequently depicted with flowers to indicate this association.<sup id="cite_ref-SOED_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOED-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Panofsky_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Panofsky-240">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> The Persephone theme is also typified by Waterhouse in his <i>Narcissus</i>, the floral <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motif</a> by <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Scorel" title="Jan van Scorel">van Scorel</a> and the landscape by <a href="/wiki/Van_Gogh" class="mw-redirect" title="Van Gogh">Van Gogh</a>\'s <i>Undergrowth</i>.\n</p><p>Narcissi first started to appear in western art in the <a href="/wiki/Late_middle_ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Late middle ages">late middle ages</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Panel_paintings" class="mw-redirect" title="Panel paintings">panel paintings</a>, particularly those depicting <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion" title="Crucifixion">crucifixion</a>. For instance that of  the Westf\xc3\xa4lischer Meister in K\xc3\xb6ln in the <a href="/wiki/Wallraf-Richartz-Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Wallraf-Richartz-Museum">Wallraf-Richartz-Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Cologne_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cologne-261">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> where daffodils symbolise not only death but also hope in the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a>, because they are perennial and bloom at <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kandeler_206-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kandeler-206">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kandeler_206-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kandeler-206">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-resur_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resur-262">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stuttgart_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stuttgart-263">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><b>Narcissi in art</b></div>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="font-size:95%; line-height:120%">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 238.66666666667px"><div style="width: 238.66666666667px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" 1415\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_cultures">Eastern cultures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Eastern cultures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">Chinese culture</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_tazetta" title="Narcissus tazetta">Narcissus tazetta</a></i> subsp. <i>chinensis</i> (Chinese sacred lilies), which can be grown indoors, is widely used as an ornamental plant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOC_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOC-264">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Todt_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todt-265">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> It was probably introduced to China by Arab traders travelling the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> prior to the <a href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Song Dynasty">Song Dynasty</a> for medicinal use.<sup id="cite_ref-Zonneveld_53-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zonneveld-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOC_264-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOC-264">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Todt_265-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todt-265">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Spring-flowering, they became associated with <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Chinese New Year</a>, signifying good fortune, prosperity and good luck<sup id="cite_ref-Mengjian_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mengjian-266">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Globaltimes_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Globaltimes-267">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> and there are many legends in Chinese culture associated with <i>Narcissus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHu1989_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHu1989-268">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey1890_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey1890-269">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Gippsland_Times&#39;&#39;1946_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Gippsland_Times&#39;&#39;1946-270">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> In contrast to the West, narcissi have not played a significant part in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Garden" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Garden">Chinese Garden</a> art,<sup id="cite_ref-Beuchert_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beuchert-271">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> however, <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Mengjian" title="Zhao Mengjian">Zhao Mengjian</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Song_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Culture of the Song Dynasty">Southern Song Dynasty</a> was noted for his portrayal of narcissi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHearn2008&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidSLpZy5ouhcgCpgPA70_Zhao_Mengjian&#93;,_p._70_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHearn2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidSLpZy5ouhcgCpgPA70_Zhao_Mengjian],_p._70-272">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHearn2008&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidSLpZy5ouhcgCpgPA70_Zhao_Mengjian&#93;,_p._70:_&quot;I_pity_the_narcissus_for_not_being_the_orchid,_which_at_least_had_known_the_sober_minister_from_Chu&quot;._273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHearn2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidSLpZy5ouhcgCpgPA70_Zhao_Mengjian],_p._70:_&quot;I_pity_the_narcissus_for_not_being_the_orchid,_which_at_least_had_known_the_sober_minister_from_Chu&quot;.-273">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> Narcissus bulb carving and cultivation has become an art akin to Japanese <a href="/wiki/Bonsai" title="Bonsai">bonsai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Globaltimes_267-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Globaltimes-267">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese novel <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissu" title="Narcissu">Narcissu</a></i> contains many references to the narcissus, where the main characters set out for the famed narcissus fields on <a href="/wiki/Awaji_Island" title="Awaji Island">Awaji Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAwaji2006&#91;httpwwweonetnejp~t-suisenindexhtml_Awaji_Tachikawa_Narcissus_Farmland&#93;_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAwaji2006[httpwwweonetnejp~t-suisenindexhtml_Awaji_Tachikawa_Narcissus_Farmland]-274">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAwaji2006&#91;httpwwwcityminamiawajihyogojpsoshikishoukousuisenkyouhtml_Nada_Kurokawa_Narcissus_Field&#93;_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAwaji2006[httpwwwcityminamiawajihyogojpsoshikishoukousuisenkyouhtml_Nada_Kurokawa_Narcissus_Field]-275">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_culture">Islamic culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Islamic culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Narcissi are one of the most popular garden plants in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_culture" title="Islamic culture">Islamic culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchimmel199871_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchimmel199871-276">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Arab conquest of Persia</a>, the Persian ruler <a href="/wiki/Khosrau_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Khosrau I">Khosrau I</a> is said to have not been able to tolerate them at feasts because they reminded him of eyes, an association that persists to this day,<sup id="cite_ref-Schimmel165_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schimmel165-277">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> as described by the poet <a href="/wiki/Ghalib" title="Ghalib">Ghalib</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schimmel165_277-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schimmel165-277">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> The eye imagery is also found in a number of poems by <a href="/wiki/Abu_Nuwas" title="Abu Nuwas">Abu Nuwas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeisamiStarkey1998545_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeisamiStarkey1998545-278">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeisamiStarkey1998583_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeisamiStarkey1998583-279">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeisamiStarkey1998662_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeisamiStarkey1998662-280">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchimmel199872_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchimmel199872-281">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> Another poet who refers to narcissi, is <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a>. Even the prophet <a href="/wiki/Mohammed" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohammed">Mohammed</a> is said to have praised the narcissus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrausch2012379_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrausch2012379-282">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><b>Narcissi in Eastern and Islamic cultures</b></div>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="font-size:95%; line-height:120%">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 128px"><div style="width: 128px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 126px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_tazetta_var_chinensis1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Narcissus_tazetta_var_chinensis1.jpg/189px-Narcissus_tazetta_var_chinensis1.jpg" width="126" height="170" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Narcissus_tazetta_var_chinensis1.jpg/284px-Narcissus_tazetta_var_chinensis1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Narcissus_tazetta_var_chinensis1.jpg/379px-Narcissus_tazetta_var_chinensis1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1712" data-file-height="2304" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Chinese Sacred Lily\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 228.66666666667px"><div style="width: 228.66666666667px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 226.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shuixiandiaoke.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Shuixiandiaoke.JPG/340px-Shuixiandiaoke.JPG" width="227" height="170" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Shuixiandiaoke.JPG/511px-Shuixiandiaoke.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/Shuixiandiaoke.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" style="width: 229.33333333333px"><div style="width: 229.33333333333px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 227.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Narcissus_poeticus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Narcissus_poeticus.jpg/341px-Narcissus_poeticus.jpg" width="228" class="gallerytext">\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_poeticus" title="Narcissus poeticus">N. poeticus</a></i> symbolising the eye in Islamic culture\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_culture">Popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The word "daffodil" has been <a href="/wiki/Daffodil_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Daffodil (disambiguation)">used widely</a> in popular culture, from Dutch cars (<a href="/wiki/DAF_Daffodil" title="DAF Daffodil">DAF Daffodil</a>) to Swedish rock bands (<a href="/wiki/The_Daffodils" class="mw-redirect" title="The Daffodils">The Daffodils</a>) to slurs against homosexuals and cross-dressers (as in the film <i><a href="/wiki/J._Edgar" title="J. Edgar">J. Edgar</a></i>, when Hoover\'s mother explains why real-life cross-dresser Barton Pinkus<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> was called "Daffy" (short for "Daffodil" and the equivalent of a <a href="/wiki/Pansy" title="Pansy">pansy</a><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>), and admonishes, "I\'d rather have a dead son than a daffodil for a son".<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>\n<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In some areas where narcissi are prevalent, their blooming in spring is celebrated in festivals. For instance, the slopes around <a href="/wiki/Montreux" title="Montreux">Montreux</a>, Switzerland and its associated <a href="/wiki/Riviera" title="Riviera">riviera</a> come alive with blooms each May (<i>May Snow</i>) at the annual Narcissi Festival.<sup id="cite_ref-Montreux_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montreux-290">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> Festivals are also held in many other countries.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Cancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Various cancer charities around the world, such as the <a href="/wiki/American_Cancer_Society" title="American Cancer Society">American Cancer Society</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ACS_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ACS-291">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> Cancer Society,<sup id="cite_ref-NZCS_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZCS-292">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> Cancer Council,<sup id="cite_ref-CCA_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCA-293">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Irish Cancer Society,<sup id="cite_ref-ICS_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICS-294">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie_Cancer_Care" class="mw-redirect" title="Marie Curie Cancer Care">Marie Curie</a> in the UK<sup id="cite_ref-MCurie_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MCurie-295">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> use the daffodil as a fundraising symbol on "Daffodil Days".<sup id="cite_ref-CCS_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCS-296">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span Narcissus</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Narcissus_(plant)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The word "daffodil" is also applied to related genera such as <i><a href="/wiki/Sternbergia" title="Sternbergia">Sternbergia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ismene_(plant)" title="Ismene (plant)">Ismene</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Fritillaria_meleagris" title="Fritillaria meleagris">Fritillaria meleagris</a></i>. It has been suggested that the word "Daffodil" be restricted to the wild species of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/N._pseudonarcissus" class="mw-redirect" title="N. pseudonarcissus">N. pseudonarcissus</a></i>. (<a href="#CITEREFHalevy1985">Halevy 1985</a>, Rees A. R. Narcissus pp. 268\xe2\x80\x93271)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Prior here refers to the poet <i>Pamphilus</i>, but it is likely he meant <i>Pamphos</i>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This <i><a href="/wiki/Carlavirus" title="Carlavirus">Carlavirus</a></i> should not be confused with the similarly named<i> Narcissus latent virus</i> which is a <i><a href="/wiki/Macluravirus" title="Macluravirus">Macluravirus</a></i>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rarely "Lentern", especially ecclesiastical usage as here, or dialect, particularly Scottish (<a href="#CITEREFMasefield2014">Masefield 2014</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=V8l8AwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA104">p. 104</a>)(<a href="#CITEREFJamieson1879">Jamieson 1879</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" 575</a>)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-227"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-227">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Asphodel of the Greek underworld has been variously associated with the white <i><a href="/wiki/Asphodelus_ramosus" title="Asphodelus ramosus">Asphodelus ramosus</a></i> (<a href="#CITEREFMacmillan1887">Macmillan (1887)</a>) or the yellow <i><a href="/wiki/Asphodeline_lutea" title="Asphodeline lutea">Asphodeline lutea</a></i> (<a href="#CITEREFGraves1949">Graves (1949)</a>), previously classified as <i>Asphodelus luteus</i>.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
